IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:           Government of Ivory Coast.
Beneficiaries:      Socifte PALMINDUSTRIE; Fonds d'Extension et de
Renouvellement (FER).
Amount:             US$13.4 million.
Terms:              20 years, including 5 years of grace; standard
variable interest rate.
Onlending Terms:    Government would on-lend US$9.7 million of the loan
proceeds to PALMINDUSTRIE at the Bank's variable
interest rate plus 3% with the same maturity and grace
period as the Bank loan to the Government, with
Government bearing the foreign exchange risk; and
US$3.7 million to FER at 9Z p.a. interest rate at the
same maturity and grace period as the Bank Loan with
the Government bearing the foreign exchange risk.
ProJect
Description:        The proposed project would be IBRD's fifth operation
in the sub-sector. It would: (i) help the Ivory Coast
meet the increasing demand for fats and edible oils
through both replacement of existing ageing oil palm
plantings aqd development of new plantings in selected
areas of smallholdings, medium-sized plantations, and
industrial plantations; (ii) further diversify and
strengthen the Ivory Coast agricultural sector through
investment in a crop with an important comparative
advantage and good long-term growth prospects; and
(iii) assist in maintenance of a tree cover in an area
where the forest cover is being depleted at an
alarming rate. The project would consist of the
following components:
(a) establishment of 11,600 ha of new PALMINDUSTRIE
industrial oil palm plantations and replanting of
about 15,000 ha of ageing plantations; (b) development
of smallholder oil palm plantings through expansion
and replanting of existing areas by about 29,100 ha
and new planting of 6,900 ha; (c) establishment of a
pilot scheme of 2,100 ha medium-sized private oil palm
plantations; (d) construction of two oil mills for
PALMINDUSTRIE with eventual capacities each of 40 tons



- viii -
PTB/hour; (e) installation of effluent treatment
systems at all PALMINDUSTRIE oil mills; (f) support of
accompanying research on oil palm agronomy and
pathology; and (g) technical assistance to
PALMINDUSTRIE.
Benefits and Risks:
The principal project benefit would be the expansion of th, oil
palm sub-sector through the production of a diversification crop In which
Ivory Coast has a clear comparatlve advantage. Apart from the
establishment of two new agro-industrial complexes In a thinly populated
area with good climatic and soil conditions, the project mphasizes
replanting, densification and new planting adjacent to existitg
agro-industrial complexes which will put existing excess processing
capacity to economic use. The project vould strengthen PALMINDUSTRIE's
financial and managerial position and, by building up a record of
profitability for PALMINDUSTRIE and outgrowers, would pave the way for
future expansion of the oil palm sub-sector based on private
entrepreneurial initiative. The project would also provide benefits by
providing employment for some 8,000 smallholders and workers In the forest
region of the country.
The major project risk would be an inadequate standard of
management of existing operations and of the large planting program at
head office and at the agro-industrial complexes. The project provides
for technical assistance to strengthen magement, training and evaluation
of the implementation of the plantlng program. Moreovers a "Program-
contract" between Government and PALMINDUSTRIE would clearly delineate the
roles and responsibilities of each party during project Implementation.
The risk of unfavorable climatic conditions and lower than expected prices
of oil palm products exists, but the economic and financial viability of
the project would only be jeopardised by substantially lower prices and
yields.

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMEKT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the RepuJlic of Ivory Coast has requested Bank
assistance in financing its Second Oil Palm Plan, a five-year project for
replanting and expansion of the oil palm sub-sector. The estimated
project cost is US$184.9 million equivalent of which PALMINDUSTRIE and
small and medium-sized outgrowers would contribute US$ 118.1 million. The
Bank and the other cofinanciers: Caisse Centrale de Cooperation
Economique (CCCE) (France), Commonwealth Development Corporation (CDC)
(UK), the European Development Fund (EDF) and the European Investment Bank
tEIB) would finance the balance of US$ 66.8 million. The Bank would
contribute US$ 13.4 million equivalent. The proposed Project would be the
Bank's fifth intervention in the sub-sector.
1.02      Oil palm has a high comparative advantage in the forest zone of
the Ivory Coast. It is one of the diversification crops (including
rubber, coconut, cotton and fruit) which must take up the growth role in
agriculture previously occupied so dominantly by cocoa and coffee over the
last two decades. For various reasons, growth of oil palm stopped in the
late seventies. Its revitalisation commenced two years ago following
action taken by the Government to introduce management improvements which
would be consolidated under the proposed Project.  Expansion of oil palm
has a signifigant part to play in the Government's Structural Adjustment
Plan to restore economic growth. Production under the proposed Second
Oil Palm Plan would help remedy the neglect of the past eight years to
meet growing local demand and increase exports. If the Plan proceeds
well, the comparative advantage of the crop would be exploited to a
greater extent than envisaged at present and the rate of planting further
increased in line with the potential for the crop. In view of the
importance of the oil palm sector to agricultural growth, the Bank's
presence is desirable to help ensure that (a) the greatly expanded program
meets its objectives; (b) the full potential of the sector is being
exploited as rapidly as prudence allows (including the examination of
different mechanisms to permit more autofinancing and less dependence on
Government resources); and (c) the industrial and agricultural reforms
being supported by SALs are effectively applied to the sector.
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      The Economy.  The impressive growth in gross domestic product
achieved during the two decades up until 1980 has been reversed during the
past four years. Since 1980, real per capita GDP has fallen with GDP
stagnating in current terms in the agricultural sector.  This
deterioration was due to a fall in the rate of volume increase of
agricultural products and alse in part to external factors such as a



- 2 -
return to more normal levels in the world market prices of primary
products such as cocoa, coffee and wood following the boom of 1978-80 as
well as a steep increase in the price of oil. The debt service burden of
the country increased as more local currency was required to service the
heavy borrowing program the Government had instigated in the 1970's,
because of the depreciation of the CFA franc and the increase in interest
rates on t'ne exterral debt. The country's growth potential has also been
affected in recent years by a combination of the following factors: (i) a
sharp deterioration in the quality of public investment programs since the
middle 1970's (large projects with high unit costs were embarked upon with
low and even negative rates of return); (ii) distortions in the system of
industrial incentives, giving little encouragement to the development of
domestic industries capable of competing with traditional foreign import
sources; and (iii) inadequate support to agricultural development,
agricultural projects receiving only a small part of the public investment
program in recent years (19X in 1984). Population in 1984 was estimated
to be 9.8 million with an average growth rate of almost 5% per annum over
the past 10 years, accentuated significantly (approximately 1.9% p.a.) by
the influx of immigrants from neighbouring countries, who today constitute
between 25-30% of the country's total population.
2.02      Economic Prospects.   In the second half of the 1980s, GDP
growth is not expected to match the rates achieved in the 1960s and 1970s.
Short term prospects have been affected by the drought of the last two
years on agricultural output. Export crop price forecasts are lower than
those attained in the boom years of the 1970s, and reserves of oil
discovered in offshore drilling are lower than expected. Financial
constraints on development over the next few years are tight, requiring
the maintenance of restrictive fiscal, investment and monetary policies.
In 1985, the debt service ratio (after rescheduling) is estimated at 28Z
of the country's export earnings, while the figure for 1986 is expected to
be around 48% in the absence of a rescheduling, underlining the critical
need for public finance restraint. To achieve sustained growth in the
medium to long term, a comprehensive adjustment strategy is required,
based on a major shift in incentives towards the production of tradeables
for export and for efficient import substitution. In this perspective, it
will be necessary to reverse the lasting discrimination against
agriculture in the system of incentives and to extend to agriculture the
industrial incentive reform launched under SAL II. This will be achieved
through the extension of the tariff and export subsidy scheme to all
agricultural activities with the exception of coffee and cocoa, given the
position of the country in international markets for these two
commodities. In particular, the overall level of incentives to
agriculture will be set so as to reflect the medium to long term
opportunity value of foreign exchange to the economy, thereby encouraging
the allocation of resources towards areas of comparative advantage for the
country. Accordingly, among agricultural activities, high priority should
be given to crops enjoying low domestic resource cost coefficients such as
oil palm, rubber, and cotton, which also allows for increasing diversifi-
cation, away from traditional coffee and cocoa. After the poor
performance of 1983 and 1984, agricultural growth is expected to improve
in 1985 and to continue increasing during the second half of the decade
witL improved productivity of public sector Investments.



-3-
B. The Agricultural Sector
2.03      The past growth of the Ivory Coast economy has been due in large
part to the development of the agricultural sector. For at least the next
decade, the country's future growth is expected to remain tied to the
continued progress made by this sector. Agriculture is the key to more
balanced regional and income development policies with presently about 60%
of the population depending directly on farming as a source of livelihood.
The bulk of agricultural production comes from some 900,000 smallholder
farms. While the number of cash crops which have been encouraged over
recent years has increased - with new emphasis on oil palm, coconuts,
rubber, cotton and sugar - cocoa and coffee production, which represent
23% and 5Z of total world supply respectively, still dominate the
agricultural economy and together contribute 75% of total foreign exchange
earnings from the sector. The two crops still represent 90% of the annual
new tree crop plantings (80,000 ha). Agricultural development based on
export crops in the 1960's and early 1970's was characterised by (a) the
exploitation of favorable world markets; (b) the establishment of
appropriate extension and research infrastructure under the coordination
of well-staffed development agencies; and (c) the stimulation of cash crop
production by realistic producer price policies. Over recent years,
however, agricultural development agencies have become less efficient with
(a) growing fixed costs and heavy staff budgets; (b) marketing of the
crops for which they are responsible turned over to separate Government
agencies; and (c) generally, an increasing remoteness from the research
agencies. Regulated producer price policies have also generated
increasingly larger margins for the Treasury at the expense of the farmer.
2.04      In recognition of certain disadvantages inherent in a policy of
development dependent upon a limited number of export crops, and the
regional disequilibrium within the country which this entails, the
Government announced, in 1980. a new orientation for its agricultural
development. The principal points included (i) a renewed intention to
increase diversification of export crops to ensure an improved stability
in export earnings; (ii) a search for ways to achieve food crops
self-sufficiency; (iii) promotion of smallholder agriculture and
encouragement to young farmers to remain on the land through improved
productivity incentives and the introduction of more modern agricultural
production techniques, including mechanisation; and (iv) encouragement of
the agricultural cooperative movement. This new policy was to be
accompanied by a harmonisation of price, tax and subsidy policies, credit
reforms and a reorganisation of development agencies so as to provide the
farmers with a greater direct participation in the development process.
The impact of this new orientation has still to make itself felt. During
a Government reshuffle in late 1983, a new Ministry of Rural Development
was created, one of whose major objectives was the promotion of food crop
self-sufficiency. Export and plantation crops, including oil palm, remain
under the control of the Ministry of Agriculture, Water and Forests.
Recent increases in producer prices have improved returns for cocoa.



- 4 -
coffee, cotton. oil palm, rubber and rice producers. A positive decision
of significance taken in 1983 returned responsibility for local and export
marketing of palm products from the Caisse de Stabilisation et de Soutien
des Prix des Productions Agricoles (CSSPPA) to PALMINDUSTRIE, the
parastatal company responsible for oil palm and coconut production (para
2.11). This was accompanied by the recruitment of the services of a
consortium of private firms to strengthen the management of the company
(para 2.12).  Similar actions are now under consideration for the state
owned sugar industry.
C. The Forest Region
2.05      The country comprises two distinct agricultural regions, the
drier savannah in the north and the humid forest region in the south where
oil palm plantations are located. The characteristics of the latter are
given below.
Key Characteristics of Forest Region
Parameter
Area, km2                                         140,000
Population, million                                 5.7
Average farm size, ha                               6.0
Agricultural Population growth, Z p.a.              2.9
Rainfall, -mm per year.                           1,400-2,500,
long wet season.
Vegetation                                        Tropical forest.
Secondary forest.
Dominant cash crops                               Cocoa, Coffee.
Dominant food crops                               Yams, Plantains, Taro,
Rice, Maize.
The forest region has a richer resource base than the savannah, a denser
and more rapidly growing population, predominantly perennial crops, and
emerging land constraints in most areas. Dense tropical forest is the
natural vegetation (at the turn of the century it used to cover 16 million
ha) but has been largely replaced by secondary forest, bush and tree crops
leaving only about 3 million ha. The main crops are cocoa and coffee,
with a combined total of 2.3 million ha and comprising 68% of the cropped
area; food crops such as maize, rice, plantains, cassava, taro and yams
make up a quarter of the planted area. Along the coast, industrial and
smallholder plantations of oil palm (90,000 ha), coconut palm (47,000 ha),
and rubber (41,000 ha) are locally important, but still comprise only
about 5% of the total cropped area.



-5-
D. The Oil Palm Sub-Sector
2.06      The palm oil produced in Ivory Coast was, until the early
1960's, obtained from natural oil palm stands in the forest areas and from
the few plantations of selected oil palm varieties operated by the
Institut de Recherche des Huiles et Oleagineux (IRHO). From the
mid-1960's, under the First Oil Palm Plan and following the creation of
SODEPALM (para 2.11), several industrial plantations were created along
the coastal strip of Ivory Coast. The creation of smallholder plantings
around the industrial plantations started in 1966-69 and continued at a
slightly lower rate thereafter until 1976 when a new surge in smallholder
planting began. Due to tLe internal problems of PALMINDUSTRIE (para
2.11), this expansion was short-lived, lasting only to 1978 after which
planting ceased until 1983. The accelerated program now necessary is the
direct consequence of this backlog in planting.

2.07      The oil palm sub-sector now ranks fourth among the country's
major sources of agricultural products after cocoa, coffee and forestry,
providing a living to some 200,000 people. In 1983, the area under oil
palm industrial plantations was approximately 50,000 ha (1963: 3,000 ha),
with 10,000 smallholder plantlngs covering approximately 32,000 ha (1965:
approx. 3,200 ha). These plantings were established by the nucleus
estate/suallholder system at nine agro-industrial complexes. The past
planting program of industrial and smallholder plantations is suumarized



-6-
in the graph above. Palm oil production in 1983/84 was approximately
146,000 tons (1968: 3,600 tons), of which some 25-30,000 tons were
exported. In the absence of the proposed project, it is projected that
Ivory Coast will become a palm oil importer by 1993, with imports rising
to 50,000 tons or 35Z of demand by 2000 (para 5.07).
2.08      The oil palm sub-sector falls into three groups: (i)
PALMINDUSTRIE, which operates nine nucleus estates/agro-industrial
complexes averaging 6.000 ha per complex, and a few other smaller
plantations which produce crude palm oil and kernel oil; (ii) small and
medium-sized outgrowers which sell their fresh fruit bunches (FFB) to
PALMINDUSTRIE; and (iii) a local processing industry which purchases the
raw material from PALMINDUSTRIE and produces refined palm oil and soap
stock derivatives. The farmgate price of FFB paid to outgrovers is fixed
annually by Government decree and has, with few exceptions, been below the
border price equivalent of the FFB, even assuming the official exchange
rate for the CFA Franc (see the graph below).

2.09      Until 1983, CSSPPA was responsible for the marketing of
PALMINDUSTRIE's output which it sold on the export market or to the local
processing industry at a slight discount. CSSPPA surrendered a part of
the sales revenue to PALMINDUSTRIE which was sufficient only to cover
operating costs and services to smallholders. In October 1983, the
marketing function was transferred to PALMINDUSTRIE. PALMINDUSTRIE's
sales to the domestic market are now being transacted at a price which is
fixed by the Government and which, during the last two years, was mostly
between 10 and 40Z below world prices, even assuming the official rate of
exchange for the CFA Franc. The recent excess of export over domestic
crude palm oil prices has placed PALMINDUSTRIE in a conflict of interest
situation: it is more attractive for PALMINDUSTRIE to export crude palm
oil than to sell it to the local processing industry which has, in fact,
complained about inadequate supplies. Since 1983, the processing
industry's purchases from PAIMINDUSTRIE have been subject to negotiated
quotas of about 80% of the quantities purchased prior to the introduction
of such quotas. The differential between domestic and export market
prices has in addition caused a speculative demand by individual exporters
purchasing crude palm oil from PALMINDUSTRIE for sale in the export market
at the higher world price. However, the official domestic price of
refined palm oil to the consumer is substantially above world market
levels which are currently artificially low due to Malaysia's subsidies
for the export of refined palm oil. The domestic wholesale price of
refined palm oil has, in the period 1970 to 1984, been about 15-20% above
a notional world price of refined palm oil calculated from the
international crude palm oil price.
E. Institutions
PALMINDUSTRIE
2.10      The Ministry of Agriculture, Water and Forests is responsible
for plantation and export crops, including cocoa, coffee, forestry, oil
palm, coconut, cotton and rubber. PALMINDUSTRIE is a State Corporation
(Societe d'Etat) responsible for developing the oil palm and coconut
sub-sectors and the marketing of the primary products from the industrial
and smallholder plantings. Day to day management of PALMINDUSTRIE is the
responsibility of the General Manager (Directeur General) who reports to a
Board which includes representatives of the Ministries of Agriculture,
Finance, Industry, Trade, the Economic and Social Council, CSSPPA and the
privately owned Blohorn Company (the major local processing company, a
subsidiary of Unilever).   The Board of Directors meets three times a year
to determine the general policy of the company and approve the accounts,
budget and investment program. PALMINDUSTRIE's annual budget is reviewed
and approved by the Ministry through the Directorate for Budget. PLanning
and Management Control (DPBCG). PAIMINDUSTRIE employs 14,500 persons; 41
of whom are expatriate staff, 31 being directly employed and 10 provided
through contracts with UNIPLANT and IRHO (para 2.12). The book value of
its capital is CFAF 35.5 billion (US$72.4 million).



- 8 -
2.11      Evolution of PALMINDUSTRIE.  PALMINDUSTRIE in its present form
is the result of 15 years of almost continuous restructuring in the
industry. The State Corporation originally in charge of the oil palm
sector, SODEPALM vas established in 1963 and underwent an important
restructuring in 1969. The result was the creation of PALMINDUSTRIE as
owner and operator of the palm oil mills. Further restructuring, in 1977,
transferred the responsibility for marketing from SODEPALM to CSSPPA and
resulted in continued reduction in the responsibilities of SODEPALM, which
is now a food crop extension agency in the coastal and forest zone with no
involvement in oil palm or coconuts. During this period PALMINDUSTRIE
progressively assumed the oil palm and coconut plantation responsibilities
of SODEPALM. In 1978, PALMINDUSTRIE took charge of management of the
industrial plantations and, in 1983, the marketing responsibility of
CSSPPA (para 2.04). PALMINDUSTRIE had by this time very much the same
functions as SODEPALM 20 years earlier. Until 1974/75, SODEPALM had
functioned well, either meeting or exceeding production forecasts.
However, after this date, as pointed out in a recent joint evaluation by
OED and CCCE 1/, the above restructurings had a very damaging effect on
its management and financial performance and on the morale and quality of
its staff. Transfer of the marketing function from SODEPALM to CSSPPA was
a major cause of its financial difficulties since surpluses were not used
on a priority basis to meet the corporation's financing needs. The
transfer also led to rigid budgeting and financing procedures forcing
SODEPALM and its successor, PALMINDUSTRIE, into unsound pre-financing
arrangements. Head Office management became ineffective and financial
control was poor. The deterioration of the financial position is
demonstrated below by the evolution of net operating results and working
capital funds over the period 1975-82:
SODEPALM/PALMINDUSTRIE Financial Position 1975-82
(CFAF billions)

2.12      The return of marketing responsibility to PALMINDUSTRIE, the
change in its top management and provision of management staff under a
technical assistance contract (para 2.04) were major factors in the
improvement of PALMINDUSTRIE's performance (para 2.16). The technical
assistance contract with UNIPLANT, a consortium of three French companies,
(Plantations des Terres Rouges, SOCFIN and SODECI International) commenced

- 9 -
in 1983. Under the contract, the services of seven expatriates are
provided for the headquarters positions of Technical, Financial, Marketing
(part- time) and Personnel Managers and for the positions of Managers of
the Vridi Oil Seed Crushing Plant and the Central Workshop. In addition,
three expatriates from IRHO occupy the positions of Deputy General
Manager, Chief of Studies Division, and Field Inspector. These
expatriates will have a key role to play in the proposed project and an
assurance was obtained at negotiations that PALMINDUSTRIE will continue
the present arrangements for employment of personnel from an oil palm
management company and an oil palm research institution during the
implementation period (para 7.01(a)). In addition, assurances were
obtained at negotiations that (i) the Bank will be consulted on
substantial changes in PALMINDUSTRIE's organizational structure and (ii)
that the quality of senior management will be maintained (para 7.01(b)).
2.13      In connection with plans for privatization, the Government is
considering conver*ing PALMINDUSTRIE into a Societe d'Economie Mixte
(SEM), a Joint Public/Private Enterprise, which would allow private
participation in its equity. However, PALMINDUSTRIE has only recently
begun to perform satisfactorily in financial terms and it will have to
consistently show positive results and a record of increasing dividend
payments over a longer period before private investors can be expected to
take a meaningful stake in the company. The proposed project does not
therefore focus directly on privatization, but on creating conditions
conducive to privatization while addressing the most pressing need: the
implementation of the proposed planting program to cover domestic
vegetable oil requirements and increase oil exports. At negotiations, an
assurance  was obtained that Government will consult with the Bank on the
proposed arrangements for participation by any private investor in the
equity of PAMIXNDUSTRIE (para 7.01(c)). The conversion to a SEN is
desirable but not essential, provided a "Contrat Plan" (Program-contract)
can be worked out between the Government and PALMINDUSTRIE that would set
the technical and financial parameters under which the company would work
(para 4.02). Furthermore, an assurance was obtained at negotiations that
Government will provide the Bank with details of proposals for the future
legal and corporate status of PALMINDUSTRIE (para 7.01(d)).
2.14      Organisation and Management. Headquarters consist of  a central
office, central workshop, stores, and a seed crushing plant. Each of
Palmindustrie's nine agro-industrial complexes consists of an industrial
plantation and a palm oil mill and a development and extension service for
smallholders within a radius of about 20 km of the mill. The management
of Palmindustrie's plantations is generally good. The plantations have an
average yield of FFB of 10 tons/ha (range 8-14 tons/ha) and labor and
management norms compare satisfactorily with those in S.E. Asia, although
yields are 50% lower than those found in Malaysia because of less fertile
soil and more uneven distribution of rainfall. Research now taking place
with vegetative propagation of elite material promises to increase yields
by 25%.  During the past 3-4 years, PALMINDUSTRIE has developed a
management structure with a satisfactory capacity for planning,
supervision and control at the central office. The manager of each
agro-industrial complex is responsible for timely and economic



- 10 -
implementation of head office plans for production and development. Head
office establishes policy and overall plans and provides supervision,
support and control of field operations through regular visits by the
Manager of Agricultural Production and the three Production and two
Processing Inspectors. Control is also obtained through an increasingly
computerized system of annual budgets and monthly progress reports.
Decentralization measures still under implementation will further transfer
administrative responsibilities for each plantation or groups of
plantations to managers in the field, leaving at Head Office the
formulation of corporate policy, internal audit, consolidation of estate
budgets, monthly preparation of technical and financial comparative
summary reports and consolidation of annual results. Technical support,
central workshop and stores, and marketing will remain centralised.
2.15      PALMINDUSTRIE's short and medium term goals are the
consolidation of an efficient organisation able to cultivate, harvest,
process and market palm and coconut products. Further development and
refining of the management systems already instituted will take place ovor
several years under a Management Development Action Program which has
recently been developed for the establishment of management
information/cost control systems, training and personnel management. A
summary of the program is found in Annex 2-1; it shows the elements of the
program, the steps that have already been taken, those that remain to be
carried out, and the time frame within which they are expected to be
completed. A time-table for completion of the major elements of the
Action Program was submitted at negotiations and is satisfactory.
Assurances were obtained that the Action Program will be implemented in
accordance with the agreed time-table (para 7.01(e)).
2.16      Recent PALMINDUSTRIE Performance.  Following the transfer of
marketing responsibility to PALMINDUSTRIE and the radical measures taken
with regard to management and financial control, there has been a major
iuprovement in the performance of PALMINDUSTRIE. The establishment of
technical standards, strict cost control and comparative cost analysis has
led to plantations and mills being operated with increased efficiency.
Cost data prior to 1983 are unreliable but, since that date, stronger
control by Head Office over physical targets and costs has reduced
overhead and field costs. In current terms, the f.o.b. cost of production
per ton of crude palm oil was CFAF 117,150 in 1983 and CFAF 115,780 in
1984. This represented a reduction of 8% in real terms. Agricultural
costs were reduced by 15Z. Revenue and net earnings have both increased
as shown below:

Some 2,000 ha of 6,000 ha previously abandoned smallholdings are in the
process of rehabilitation under supervision of the PALMINDUSTRIE extension
services. Data relating to recent financial performance and mill
efficiency are presented In Annex 2-2.
Smallholders
2.17        In addition to oil palm industrial plantations, PALMINDUSTRIE
plays a major role in the operation and expansion of the smallholder
program, at present accounting for 40% of the total area planted with oil
palm. The smalibolder extenslon service is weak in terms of the
regularity of farm visits, the diffusion of technical messages and the
training of extension agents. As a result, average yields (6 tons/ha) are
some 40Z lower than those achieved on the estates (10 tons/ha), although
some oil palm fruit is sold in the local markets and therefore not
accounted for. Modifications to the organisation of the extension
services designed to alleviate the above weaknesses are described in para
4.05.



- 12 -
Medium-sized Plantations
2.18      The development approach employed so far has been that of the
classical nucleus estate and smallholder model. As a variant to this
approach and as in the Fourth Rubber Project (Ln. 2323-IVC of 1983), the
Government wishes to promote. as does PALMINDUSTRIE, medium-sized
plantations of between 50 and 150 ha as compared to the large size
plantations of 1,000 to 4,000 ha. The objectives of this new approach
would be (1) to provide an alternative to PALMINDUSTRIE as the sole
development agent of the sub-sector and to harness entrepreneurial
initiative; (ii) to provide an outlet for private investors interested in
the sub-sector; and (iii) to develop, in view of the limited amount of
land available for further large industrial plantations, an efficient
medium-sized plantation sub-sector with (a) lower overhead costs than
large plantations and (b) higher productivity but lower extension costs
than with the average smallholder. Unlike the smallholders, the
medium-sized plantations would be expected to be independent entities with
their own harvest and transport equipment and their own social and housing
facilities for staff and labor. Processing would be carried out in
PALMINDUSTRIE mills.
2.19      Prospective entrepreneurs would fall into four groups:
'i)    private investors who previously focussed on the urban sector,
but now seek investment opportunities in organised areas of the
rural sector;
(ii)    civil servants, singly or in groups, interc ited in involvement
in farming, after retirement at 55 years;
(iii)   young agricultural graduates;
(Iv)    groups of smallholders.
Under the Project, this new approach would be implemented over
an initial area of some 2,000 ha and is described in more detail in
Chapter III. PALMINDUSTRIE has carried out a feasibility study,
satisfactory to the Bank, on the development of this area.
The Expansion and Replanting Fund (FER)
2.20      Existing Situation.  The objective of the Expansion and
Replanting Fund is to provide short, medium and long term financing for
the production or processing of oil palm and coconut products, including
both industrial and smallholder plantings. PER past and ongoing
operations include the financing of: social infrastructure on
PALMINDUSTRIE industrial plantations; the establishment and maintenance of
suallholder and industrial oil palm and coconut plantations; and, to a
lesser extent, studies associated with the development of oil palm and
coconuts. In the past, interest rates charged by FER have been ad hoc and
generally at subsidised levels. The present policy of FER is that loans
be passed on at the rate at which the necessary funds were received by
FER. FER was created in 1964 as a Fund under Decree 64-223. In 1969,



- 13 -
under Law 69-246, FER was granted a legal existence and financial
autonomy. Decree 69-264 of the same date created a Management Committee
of three members representing the M4nistries of Agriculture, Finance and
Planning.
2.21      Under Decree 69-264 the Management Committee, which includes in
addition the Directeur General of PALMINDUSTRIE as an observer, is obliged
to meet at least once a year. Until end 1984, the Committee was served by
a Secretary General, an official of the Ministry of Agriculture who
resigned at that date and has not yet been replaced. At present, the
Director General of PALMINDUSTRIE represents the Ministry of Agriculture
on the Management Committee. The Committee is responsible for the
approval of annual budgets, activity plans, and annual reports. It is
served by a small staff which includes the Secretary General, a part-time
secretary and an official of the Ministry of Finance, paid by FER,
ma4ntains the accounts and another official from the same Ministry
supervises budgets and accounts. The budget, activity plans and annual
reports are prepared by the Secretary General of the Committee. Proper
financial statements of FER have been prepared and a satisfactory audit of
FEM's accounts up to end 1984 is available.   However, the existing
accounting system is not fully satisfactery and requires improvement, as
do the organisational, management and operating arrangements. On the
other hand, transactions between FER and PALMINDUSTRIE are adequately
recorded in the PALMINDUSTRIE accounts.  Repayment of loans from FER to
PALMINDUSTRIE is on schedule.
2.22      Proposed Measures. Government intends to endow FER  Lith a legal
status which vould allow Government to onlend externally raised funds to
the PER and allow the FER to build up reserves. Under the future legal
structure, the FER would be maintained as a small, simple organisation but
independent in its policy formulation, accounting and control from
PALMfIDUSTRIE. Establishment of FER under new statutes satisfactory to
the Bank would be a condition of Loan effectiveness (para 7.02(a)).
The National Agricultural Development Bank
2.23      The National Agricultural Development Bank (BEDA) was created in
1968 to become the major source of credit to the agricultural sector.
BNDA's active participation in the development of the agricultural sector
is however impeded by severe problems at management level, a deficient
management information system and an unsatisfactory record nf credit
recovery.  Management of BNDA has recently been taken over by t.'e Central
Bank. At this stage, BNDA cannot satisfactorily participate in the
Project. However, the Bank is already involved with BNDA in the Ivory
Coast Fourth Rubber Project (Ln. 2323-IVC of 1983) and, once the present



- 14 -
troubles of BNDA are resolved, will be actively working with Government on
the preparation of a project for the development of agricultural credit
institutions.
The Ministry of Education and Scientific Research
2.24       The Ministry is in charge of research in the oil palm
sub-sector. The main research station is at La Me, with substations at
Dabou and Sassandra. The quality of research is high and impressive
progress is being made in the advanced field of vegetative propagation.
Due to the present difficult economic situation, the research stations in
Ivory Coast ar^ suffering from a shortage of operating funds. A National
Agricultural Research Project now under preparation for assistance by the
Bank would deal with this and other problems of the research system.
Long Term Sub-sector and Institutional Strategy
2.25      Given the potential for further expansion of oil palm and
coconuts and the need to export their products in the context of national
economic growth and diversification away from cocoa and coffee, Government
and PALMINDUSTRIE propose that a study be undertaken to define the best
strategy to reach these objectives. The present organisational pattern
and arrangements of the sub-sector could continue but other alternatives
could also be examined. So far, expansion has depended almost entirely on
Government intervention in the sector. Greater private involvement in
both large and medium-sized plantations would permit the sub-sector to
mobilize more capital than is likely to be available from Government
sources, and the possible forms of private capital investment in the
sub-sector should be identified. Privatising existing Government
investments in PALMINDUSTRIE is one possibility. Splitting up
PALMINDUSTRIE into smaller, more flexible and dynamic units able to
undertake further expansion programs is another alternative. Finally, the
feasibility of establishing joint ventures between Government and private
plantation operators and investors operating in parallel to PALMfIDUSTRIE
could be examined. The selection of areas and soils for future expansion
would also require careful consideration, particularly in relation to
environmental effects where clearing of forest is proposed. The
unfavorable trend in rainfall distribution over the past 20 years is such
that expansion should be concentrated on alluvial and low lying soils with
high water tables. North Tamabo included in the project would be the
f' st development of this type.  The above study should therefore include
a soil survey to determine the location and suitability of soils with a
high oil palm and coconut yield potential, taking into account the
suitability for rubber, also an important diversification crop for vhich
it is proposed that the Bank provide further financing in the future. The
study would be the basis for Government plans for the future development
of the tree crop sector overall.  Arrangementr for the implementation of
the study are in para 3.17.



- 15 -
F. Bank Involvement in the Oil Palm Sub-Sector
2.26      Between 1969 and 1978 Bank contributions to the oil palm/coconut
sub-sector in the Ivory Coast totalled US$46.7 million providing partial
financing for four projects with a global estimated cost of US$96.0
million. In addition to the Bank and parallel CCCE interventions in the
sector, EDF is currently contributing US$12.2 million to the financing of
a three-year project (1983-85) aimed at replanting and extension of 14,000
ha of both industrial oil palm plantations and smallholdings. The above
four Bank-financed projects made an important contribution to the
agricultural infrastructure on which the oil palm and coconut programs
were based. Generally, the OED Evaluation Report (para 2.11) acknowledged
the physical achievements under the four Bank supported proj ects but
identified important smallholder problems, particularly with coconuts,
related to unsatisfactory smaslholder selection, poor extension and
inadequate prices. The present project will address these issues.
III.   THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
3.01      The major objective of the proposed project, which covers Ivory
Coast's Second Oil Palm Plan, is to increase the country's palm oil
production to meet the rapidly increasing domestic demand for fats and
oils and to diversify the export revenue base of the country, both through
replacement of ageing oil palm plantations and development of new
plantings in selected areas cf smalIholdings, medium-sized plantations,
and industrial plantations. Successful implementation of the Plan would
pave the way for an increased rate of planting in line with the potential
for the crop.    Furthermore, the forest area of the country is being
rapidly depleted and environmental considerations require maintenance of
the forest and tree crop area to the extent feasible. The oil palm is a
tree crop of major Importance which can be grown economically by both
industrial plantations and outgrowers, small and mediun'-sized.
B. Rationale for Bank Involvement
3.02      The period during which the Project will be executed is a
critical one, upon which depends the future capacity of the sub-sector to
generate sufficient production of a key food item for the domestic market
and to develop a third major export crop besides cocoa and coffee in the
forest zone. Although PALI4NDUSTRIE has achieved major improvements during
the early 1980s, the achievement of the proposed larger project is very
dependent on a core group of nationals and expatriates at head office. A
major program of Ivorianisation has been carried out over the past two
years and many of the Ivorians in senior positions, although dynamic and
motivated, do not yet have a long experience. That PALMIIDUSTRIE has
recovered dramatically over the past few years does not necessarily mean
that it has achieved full efficiency nor that this improved performance
would continue without the Project. Experience in the 1970's shows how
quickly PALMflDUSTRIE can deteriorate. Furthermore, the expansion



- 16 -
envisaged is a much bigger task than the rehabilitation and area expansion
of the early 1980s, although similar to that of the 1960s. The technical
support and discipline associated with a Bank proj ect are considereu vital
for the implementation of the project within the tight time frame
necessary. The Government desires Bank participation for this very
reason. Apart from the question of project discipline, it will be
necessary during project implementation to define the strategy for future
development of the sub-sector (para 2.25). The Project will be a vehicle
for (i) an adequate dialogue on strategy for this important sector between
Government, PALMINDUSTRIE and the Bank; and (ii) ensuring continued
compatibility between agricultural and industrial policies in a leading
area of agro-industrial activity. The preparation of this Project was
financed from the Project Preparation Facility.
C. The Project Area
3.03      The project will be located in the tropical forest zone of the
southern Ivory Coast (see Map). The new plantings in the project will be
mostly confined to the south-eastern and south-western parts of the
country which are well suited to oil palm development. The new industrial
and smallholder plantings in the south-west will be located at Blidouba
and Neka. These are on the Tabou to Ta. Government road, which will be
upgraded under the proposed Second HR'ghway Sector Loan. Tabou is
connected with San Pedro por  aGa palm oil bulking facilities by a
Government road of 104 km, which will be rehabilitated and improved under
the ongoing First and proposed Second Highway Sector Loans. Because of
their importance to the project, assurances were obtained at negotiations
that Government will maintain these roads in a condition which will allow
transport of the produce of Blidouba and Neka all year around (para
7.01(f)). The main technical characteristics of the area are summarized
in the table below. Details are in Annex 3-1.
Project Area - Technical Characteristics
Geographical location       Tropical forest zone of southern
Ivory Coast at a latitude around 6ON.
Water deficit               South-west:  100 mm/year
South-east:  200   t
Center    : 300   "

August/September to October/November.

3.04     Despite less favorable climatic and soil conditions in the
central south near to Abidjan, modest planting will continue there, mainly
in the context of the smallholder program. Because of a substantial
demand for fresh fruit for artisanal production of fresh "red palm oil"
for domestic consumption, there is a need for oil palm plantings in the
central south area. SmAllholders in these areas sell part of their
production to PALMINDUSTRIE and a smaller part to the parallel markets for
fresh fruit and for artisanal fresh oil production. Without these central
south plantings, the suallholder, even in areas far from Abidjan, would be
tempted to sell to the parallel market for FFB instead of to
PALMINDUSTRIE. Not only would this situation reduce PALKINDUSTRIE's
supply of FFB, it would undermine the current smallholder credit system
based on a cess on the FFB sold to and collected by PALMINDUSTRIE.
3.05     Land Tenure. PALMINDUSTRIE has negotiated land rights with both
Government and the local population in the south-west where new plantings
are planned at Blidouba and Neka. At North Tamabo the land has already
been declassified for the use of Societe pour le Developpement des
Plantations Forestiares (SODEFOR). Arrangements for transfer from SODEFOR
have been made. At negotiations, PALNIUSTRIE provided documetation
satisfactory to the Bank, demonstrating for each land area, (i) the status
of occupation; (ii) the rates of compensation to be provided in the case
of acquisition; and (iii) evidence that the land is available for use by
PALMINDUSTRIE.
3.06     Labor. The availability of labor will be adequate for areas to
be replanted, where labor will be released from former maintenance and
harvesting activities. The labor requirement for the new industrial
plantings is expected to reach a peak of 3,700 man years in 1989 and is
not anticipated to be a problem. Criteria for smallholder selection will
include labor availability.
D. Project Description
3.07     The proposed project will finance a five year program of
planting and also the associated additional oil palm processing capacity.
The project will consist of the following components:
Industrial Plantations
(a) establishment by PALMINDUSTRIE of 11,600 ha of new industrial
oil palm plantations in the south-west, at Blidouba (3,000 ha),



- 18 -
Neka (6,000 ha), and in the center at North Tamabo (2,000 ha)
and Bolo (600 ha);
(b)  replanting of about 15,000 ha of ageing industrial oil palm
plantations spread over the entire PALMINDUSTRIE area;
Smallholder Plantings
(c) expansion and replanting of existing smallholder oil palm
plantings by about 29,100 ha around all the existing
PAIMINDUSTRIE plantations;
(d) establishment of about 6,900 ha new smallbolder oil palm
plantings at Ehania, Dabou, North Tamabo, Blidouba and Neka;
Medium-sized Plantations
(e) establishment of a pilot scheme of medium-sized oil palm
plantations, amounting to 2,100 ha at Blidouba, Neka and North
Tamabo;
Oil Mills
(f)  construction of two palm oil mills with eventual capacities each
of 40 tons FFBIhour;
Effluent Treatment
(g) establishment of effluent treatment systems at all PALMINDUSTRIE
oil mills;
Research
(h) support for accompanying research on oil palm agronomy and
pathology;
Technical Assistance
(i) technical assistance to PALMINDUSTRIE for project
implementation, the evaluation of project progress and the
development of a training program.
E. Detailed Features
3.08      The Planting Program.  The total planting program amounts to
close to 65,000 ha, equivalent to about 75% of the present planted area of
oil palm. The detailed planting program is in Annex 3-2. The program
consists of (i) new planting in new areas; (ii) new planting within
existing areas (densification); and (lii) replanting. The program is
summarized graphically below according to project year and type of

- 20 -
planting. These physical targets are considered feasible since (i) the
overall program will be spread among twelve sites where - with the
exception of the two new locations at Neka and Blidouba - infrastructure,
staff, facilities and equipment already exist; (ii) the actual area
planted either through new plantings and/or replantings vill not be more
than 10-15X per annum of the present hectarages at the ten existing sites;
(iii) the number of smallholder participants which, at 8,000. is
relatively small from a management view point in relation to the number of
sites; and (iv) the agricultural techniques are well established.
Technical aspects are in Annex 3-1. The target of 12,000 ha planting in
the current EDF project will be surpassed by over 15% (para 2.16).
Nursery facilities have been established for a 5,400 ha planting program
in 1985 and the planting material will be available on schedule.
3.09      Industrial Plantations.  New industrial planting is constrained
by the availability of land close to existing collection and processing
centers. The proposed area of 11,000 ha for new industrial planting under
the project will be limited to two sites in the south- west, Neka and
Blidouba. and to new areas near existing sites at North Tamabo and Bolo in
the center (see Map). New oil mills of eventual 40 tons FFB/hour capacity
will be established only at Neka and Blidouba (para 3.13).
3.10      The industrial plantation replanting program has been drawn up
to cover the next 25 years. The short term objective is to compensate for
the backlog in replanting resulting from the failure to replant in
1977-81, by replanting 15,00) ha of ageing plantations. The longer term
objective is to even out the age composition of the plantations, to reach
a relatively constant annual rate of replanting. These objectives will be
achieved by the proposed replanting program, which has been designed to
balance the rate of replanting on the one hand and the level of production
on the other. The replanting program also comprises densification on some
plantations. Densification will increase the hectarage within an area
served by existing infrastructure, and the capacity utilization of
existing oil mills (on such plantations), which are presently
underutilized.
3.11      Smallholder Plantings. The total smallholder program proposed
covers 36,000 ha of which 29,100 ha of replantings and extensions will be
planted in areas served by existing infrastructure. Smallholder plantings
in new areas, amounting to 6,900 ha, will be established at Ehania, Dabou,
North Tamabo, Blidouba and Neka. At Dabou, palms will not be planted In
the low yielding areas, termed savannah. Some 8,000 smallholders, with an
average of 4.5 ha of new palms each, will participate in the program of
whom some 50% will be farmers who had honored the terms of their contract
with PALMINDUSTRIE in the previous projects and 50% will be new
participants.
3.12      Medium-sized Plantations. Medium sized plantations of between
50 and 150 ha will be established at Blidouba, Neka and North Tamabo.
Because this type of operation has not been tried on a large scale, the
proposed program of 6,600 ha will be implemented in two phases. Planting
of the first phase involving about 15-20 planters and 2,100 ha will be



- 21 -
carried out over three years (1986-88) and vill be followed In late 1988
by an in-depth review. The efficient establishment and development of the
above plantings will be a pre-condition for the subsequent Implementation
of the full program. The financing of this second phase is not included
in the project, but CCCE would be prepared to consider providing the
necessary additional financing should the pilot component prove
successful.
3.13      Oil Mills. Two new oil mills of eventual 40 tons FF5/hour
capacity and equipped with effluent disposal systems will be established
on the Blidouba and Neka sites. Construction of the first phase of 20
tons to be financed under the project, will start In 1988 and be
completed by 1991. The second phase of an additional 20 tons If
necessary, would be completed in 1994. The average peak month production
at the PALMINDUSTRIE mills Is 12.3Z of the annual production. However,
capacity requirements of the Blidouba and Neka mills have been calculated
for reasons of prudence, on the basis of a peak month production of 151.
Mill operation during this month was assumed to be 25 days at 20 hours per
day (500 hours). The existlg oil mills will cope with the expected
production at all other stes.
3.14      Effluent Tretment. Liquid effluents are discharged from the
mills Into the surrounding natural water systems,  The effluent traps
inetalled at the mills are ineffective with the effluent effect at the
average mill amounting to approximately 15 kg SOD/ton m, which is high.
The problem has been intensively studied by PALXMINUSTRIIE tho Ministry of
Industry's Environmental Protection Directorate and the Bank's Environment
Adviser and proposals have been made for treatment of effluent using the
anerobic/facultative pond system or equivalent process (Annez 3-3).
Finae will be provided under the project for construction over the
project period, of effective effluent treatment systes at all existing
mills under a phased program. Assurances were obtained at negotiations
that PALMINDUSTRIE will installs during the project implmeantation period,
tretamnt systems at the new and existing mills according to a suitable
tlmetable to be presented to the Bank for agreoent before December 31,
1986 (para 7.01(g)).
3.15       A prototype pond system will be installed at one of the
existing mills with a critical effluent problem and will provide the
design data for the system at Blidouba and Neka and at the existing
PALMINDUSTRIE mills. Designs with estimates of capital and operating
costs will be provided to the Bank.
3.16      Agricultural Research. A omll program of research directed to
project needs will be implemented by 1110 over a period of five years. If
necessary, the program will be continued under the proposed National



- 22 -
Agricultural Reararch Project (para 2.24). Under the project, finance
will be provided to investigate problems of maintenance of soil fertility
in replanted oil palms and Pusarium root wilt disease. Satisfactory
technical proposals from PALNINDUSTRIE and IRHO have been received for the
above two areas of research envisaged. A contract specifying the work to
be done, personnel to be employed and the budget, will be approved by the
Bank before disbursement against eligible costs of this research (para
7.03).
3.17      Study of the Oil Palm and Coconut Sub-sector.  The study of the
sub-sector (para 2.25) will be carried out during the project
implementation period by the planning staff of Government and
PALMINDUSTRIE. Draft terms of reference for the study are presented in
Annex 3-4.
3,18      Training of PALMINDUSTRIE Staff. Training will be an integral
part of PALMINDUSTRIE's Management Development Action Program (para 2.15).
Under this program, the emphasis on training will be greatly increased,
the scope of training will be expanded and training organised on a
systematic basis with the assistance of the training consultant (para
3.19). At present, training is largely restricted to technical aspects
of agriculture. It will be widened to include crop processing,
administration, finance, commercial operations and management (Annex 3-5).
The rate of Ivorianisation in recent years has been rapid, with the number
of expatriates declining from 110 in 1979, 70 in 1982 to 41 in 1984.
There is no expectation that a major proportion of the presently
expatriate staffed higher management positions will be Ivorianised during
the implementation period, and certaiu expatriates may be required to
assist with the Third Plan which, it is anticipated, will be proposed
following the Strategy Study (para 2.25). The objective of the training
program, however, will be to develop Ivorian staff capabilities to the
standards required to fill most of the expatriate held
positions over the next 5-10 years.
3.19      Technical Assistance.  The project will provide financing for
the proposed technical assistance under the existing contracts with an oil
palm plantation management company and an oil palm research institution,
satisfactory to the Bank (para 2.12). Thore are two main objectives in
the provision of this technlcal assistance: (a) to assist in the planning,
direction and control of project implementation; and (b) to assist in the
implementation of the Action Program for the Management Development
Program (para 2.15). The total man months provided under the tec'znical
assistance contracts will be 546 (Annex 3-6). In addition, short-term
assistance will be provided in specific technical areas required to
support sub-sectoral and project activities for: (a) two visiting
advisers, one technical and one financial required for evaluation of
project progress (Annex 3-7); (b) establishment of PALMINDUSTRIE's
training programs and for training of trainers, a training consultant
(para 3.18); and (c) other purposes as the nued arises and as agreed



- 23 -
between PALINDUSTRIE and the Bank. This short-term assistance will total
68 man months. At negotiations an assurance was given that Bank approval
will be obtained prior to appointment of each of the speciallets for short
term technical assistance. In addition, assurances were given that
PALMINDUSTRIE will consult with the Bank before introducing modifications
into its medium-term technical assistance contracts (para 7.01(h)).

3.23      US$40.9 million of the financing will be onlent to PALMINDUSTRIE
for the following components: industrial plantations, oil mills, technical
assistance, effluent treatment, research and PPF. Government will take
the foreign exchange risk. US$25.9 million will be onlent to FER for the
financing of small and medium sized plantations. The Government will bear
the foreign exchange risk. The Bank loan will be made to Government and
onlent: (i) to PAUIINDUSTRIE at the same maturity and grace period as the
Bank loan and at the Bank's variable rate plus 3Z; and (1l) to PER at 91,
and the same maturity and grace period as the Bank loan. Cofinanciers
will lend to Government and to PER on their standard terms. The weighted
average of Bank and cofinanciers (CCCE, CDC and EDF) loans to PER will be
about 5Z. The conclusion of subsidiary loan agreements satisfactory to
the Bank between Government and PALMIUDUSTRIE and Government and FER will
be a condition of effectiveness (para 7.02 (c)).
3.24      Total external financing will amount to 43% of project costs net
of taxes and duties and 362 of gross project costs. PALKENDUSTRIE will
finance 76Z of the industrial plantations and 55% of the oil mills, for a
total of CPAP 38.6 billion (US$78.7 million). This amount corresponds to
35Z of PALMINDUSTRIE's projected net positive cumulative cash flow in the
period 1985-90 before project investment costs, allocation to dividends,
free funds and external financing (para 5.19). Smallholders will
contribute CFAF 13.3 billion (US$27.1 million) with own and hired labor
and small tools; FER will contribute to the smallholder plantations In the
amount of CFAP 5.2 billion (US$10.6 million), out of its current
resources; and medium-sized planters will contribute CFAF 0.8 billion
(US$1.7 million) in cash (about 25% of estimated plantation cost under the
pilot scheme) with FER channelling about CPAP 2.5 billion (US$5.1 million)
of external funds for the necessary complementary financing. The project



- 26 -
will finance the eligible project cost of this category up to 1990.
Beyond this date, promoters of these plantations would be referred to the
BNDA or commercial banks for their financing needs.
3.25      Although not included as a project cost, the Government will be
required to pay about US$13.2 million in interest payments on external
financing during the five project lmplementation years, 1986-90.

3*27       All procurement will be grouped and combined with requirements

valued at US$200,000 equivalent or more will be procured through
international competitive bidding. Such contracts will comprise mainly
fertilizers, chemicals, plastic bags, wire netting, land cleartng
equipment, vehicles and oil mills. Contracts worth less than US$200,000
equivalent but more than US$75,000 equivalent will be procured by local
competitive bidding and will comprise inputs, building and construction
materials, fuel, effluent treatment plant and recurrent cost expenditures.
Because of the dispersed nature of the program bcth geographically and
time-vise local competitive bidding procedures or local shopping for
contracts less than US$75,000 equivalent will be employed. Where local
shopping is required a minimum of three quotations vill be obtained or, in
the case of germinated seeds a contract will be negotiated with the only
supplier, IRHE0. Road construction, land clearing and building work will be
carried out on force account which PALMINDUSTRIE has demonstrated it can
achieve more effectively than by contracting out. The total value of goods
and services to be procured through ICB will be about US$62.0 million (34Z
of total project costs); through LCB, about US$28.0 million (15%); and
through other methods, about US$95.0 million (51X) of which about US$66.0
million for staff and labor costs, for which bidding procedures are
Inapplicable. Research will be carried out by IRHO under a contract
satisfactory to the Bank negotiated between PALMINDUSTRIE and the Ministry
of Research. Short term technical assistance (68 man months) will be
recruited through procedures acceptable to the Bank.
Procurement for elements financed by the other donors will be
according to Bank procedures with the following variations:
CCCE -    restricts procurement to elements produced in the franc
zone if comparable items exist with origin in the franc
zone;

opportunity to bid;
EDF  -    normally requires procurement from EEC or ACP states,
and       but prepared to follow Bank procurement guidelines when
EIB       co-financing, subject to final contract being from an
eligible source;

follows:
CCCE -    agricultural tractors, planting costs, research,

CDC  -    planting costs, and oil mills;
EDP  -    for smallholders only, planting costs;
EIB  -    for industrial plantations only, land clearing
equipment, planting costs and oil mills.

H. Disbursements
3.28      The schedule of disbursements is based on the Ivory Coast
agricultural projects disbursements profile amended for factors known to
cause divergence. Project disbursements show much the same pattern as the
standard profile (Annex 3-9), but start at a higher level because of
retroactive financing of land clearing equipment amounting to US$1.4
million. Retroact1ve financing is required to cover: (i) part of the cost
incurred during the initial 1986 pre-planting period, between April 1, 1985
and loan effectiveness anticipated for December 1, 1985; and (1i) the PPF.
Disbursements will also end one year earlier than the profile in view of
PAIMIDUSTRIE's demonAstrated Implementation capacity.  The Bank loan
disbursed over a seven year period, will be as showv below.
