planted, to be claimed once for each specific area planted under the
project at the end of the first planting year of each area. Payment
certificates for the areas planted each year will be prepared by
PALMINDUSTRIN and verified by PALMINDUSTRIE field inspectors. Planting
costs will be financed in parallel with CCCE, CDC, EDF and EIB.
Disbursements for expenditures whose value is less than US$20,000 will be
made agarist statements of expenditure, the documentation for which will be
retained in PALMThDUSTRIE's offices for inspection by supervision missions.
To improve the efficient and timely implementation of the Project, the Bank
will deposit, after loan effectiveness, an initial sum of US$750,000 in a
revolving fund to finance eligible expenditures corresponding to Bank's
share of the average four month's Project financing needs. The Bank funds
will be deposited in CFAF in a special account with the Caisse Autonome
d'Amortissement (CAA) and will be channelled as needed to the Project
through PALMINDUSTRIE's and FER's individual accounts with CAA.
PALMINDUSTRIE's, FER's, planters' and cofiuanciers' counterpart funds will
be timely secured through appropriate allocations. The Bank vil. replenish
the revolving fund as requested upon receipt of satisfactory evidence that
expenditures made from it were eligible for financing out of the loan. A
replenishment request will normally be sent by CAA to the Bank when about
502 of the revolving fund has been distributed, i.e., every two months.
Should any disbursement made from the special account not be acceptable to
h-e Bank, PER or PALMINDUSTRIE will be requested to deposit the
corresponding ineligible amount into the special account or, if the Bank so
requests, refund to the Bank, through CAA, an amount equal to the amount
not eligible. No further deposit into the special account will be made by
the Bank when the total unwithdrawn amount of the part of the loan that is
expected to be disbursed at loan closure will be equal to US$1.0 million.
If it is determined that any amount outstanding in the special account is
not required to cover further payments for eligible expenditures, the
Government will refund to the Bank such amount outstanding in the special
account. Assurances on the above points have been obtained at
negotiations. Opening of the Special Account with CAA will be a condition
of effectiveness of the Loan (para 7.02 (d))
I. Reporting, Accounts and Audits
3.30      PALMINDUSTRIE accounts are well kept; accounts have been received
for the year to September 30, 1983 and audited accounts up to September 30,
1984 are under preparation. PALMINDUSTRIE will submit to its Board of
Directors for approval and to Goverment and the Bank for information,
consolidated annual budgets at least three months before the start of the
financial year (para 7.01(i)). PALMIIDUSTRIE will submit quarterly reports
to the Bank including detailed financial progress reports for each of the
project sites comparing planned and actual results. FER will submit
quarterly statements including detailed financial progress reports.
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that PALMINDUSTRIE and FER will
(i) maintain appropriate records and accounts; (ii) have their accounts
audited by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank; and (iii) submit
to the Bank, audited annual accounts within six months of the end of their
financial year (para 7.01(j)).  The auditors will provide a long form
report to management of PALMINDUSTRIE and of FER and to the Bank with



- 30 -
detailed comments on the filnacial, general and cost accounting systems.
In addition, the auditors will provide a certificate to the effect that
all goods and services acquired under the loan were acquired from qualified
countries, that they have been paid for and have been used for purposes of
the project and that statements of expenditure have becn verified. Within
six months after the loan closing date, PALMINDUSTRIE will prepare or have
prepared a completion report covering all components of the project for
submission to the Bank. (para 7.01(k)).
IV. ORGANISATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. General
4.01      The project will be implemented within the present institutional
framework of the Ivory Coast under the auspices of the Kinistry of
Agriculture which has overall responsibility for the sub-sector. The
industrial plantings and mill construction will be executed by
PALMINDUSTRIE, the smallholder plantings by smallholders themselves under
the supervision of the PAIMINDUSTRIE extension service and the medium-sized
plantations by the owners. The existing arrangements for the organisation
and management of PALMINDUSTRIE are operating satisfactorily and will be
continued. The General Manager of PALMINDUSTRIE will be responsible for
project implementation. The existing technical assistance provided by an
oil palm plantation management company and an oil palm research institution
will be continued (para 2.12). The PALMIXDUSTRIE organisation chart and
details of organisation and management are in Annexes 4-1 and 4-2
respectively.
B. Industrial Plantations
4.02      The Contrat Plan.  The technical and financial parameters within
which PALMINDUSTRIE will conduct its Industrial operations during the
project implementation period will be established in a contract between
PALMINDUSTRIE and Government termed "Contrat Plan".  Terms of reference for
the "Contrat Plan" are in Annex 4-3. The establishment of objectives and
conditions of execution under the "Contrat Plan" will (i) provide
Government with the means to monitor PALMINDUSTRIE's performance; and (ii)
ensure PALMINDUSTRIE's managerial and financial autonomy. Government will
carry out jointly with PALMINDUSTRIE an annual review of execution of the
"Contrat Plan" not later than four months following the end of the
PALMINDUSTRIE financial year. Minutes of the review meeting will be made
available to the Bank for comment. The draft "Contrat Plan" between
PALMINDUSTRIE and Government was submitted for review by the Bank at
negotiations. Signature by the parties concerned of a "Contrat Plan"
satisfactory to the Bank is a condition of Loan effectiveness (para
7.02(e)). Any substantive change in the "Contrat Plan" will require the
Bank's prior agreement (para 7.01(1)).
4.03      Management.  The Improved management system. already introduced
are satisfactory and will be further developed during the implementatiou
period under the Management Development Action Program (para 2.15).
PALMINDUSTRIE head office staff will continue to prepare annual land
clearing and planting programs specifying the precise areas to be planted



- 31 -
and the calendar to be followed in conjunction with the Managers of the
Agro-Industrial Complexes.
C. Smallholders and Medium-sized Plantations.
4.04      Suallholder Plantings.  Smallholders will be selected on the
basis of criteria which vill include age, land tenure, family composition,
availability of labor, presence on the holding, distance from the mill (20
km or less) and suitability of soil and terrain (thus benefitting from
previous project experience and overcoming criticisms made in the OED
Report). In the case of candidates who had already participated in
previous projects, a record of efficient oil palm managament will be
required. Government policy is that smallholders should have six ha of oil
palms. New participants will therefore plant up to six ha generally over a
three-year period. Previous participants will plant an area sufficient to
bring their total oil palm plantings up to six ha. On average such
smallholders will plant three ha each. Participants will sign the standard
agreement with PALMINDUSTRIE stating the obligations of both parties. The
role of PALMINDUSTRIE will be extension, supply of lnputs, collection of
FMB and payments to the smallholders for their FFM. Smallholders will be
expected to provide their own labor as they have done in the past.
4.05      Extension organisation will be modified to Incorporate, where
suitable, the principles of the Training and Visit System but adapted to
the technical requirements of the oil palm. Emphasis will be placed upon
Improvement of the tachnical capability of extension agents and the
systematic organisation of their field visits. Attention will be given to
the accurate recording of farmer yields. Four subject matter specialists
(SM) will be appointed and will receive regular training from the research
staff of IRBO. The time of SMS will be divided between training and
exposure to research, field visits and training of extension agents.
4.06      Medium-sized Plantations.  Each prospective operator of a medium-
sized plantation vill be requested to submit a project plan and will be
required to mest at least the following criteria:
(i) there should be no less than 50 ha available for the plantations;
(ii)  a satisfactory title must be produced providing evidence of
ownership of the land by the prospective operator;
(III) where the promotor is not h1imself technically qualified or
available to operate the plantation, employment of a suitable
technician would be required;
(iv) prior to entering lito a development agreement with PER and
PALMINDUSTRIE for each area to be planted, the owner must supply
evidence that he can provide at least 25Z of the total
establishment and development cost for each area;
(v) the proposed planting site must be within a 20 km radius of
existing oil mills.



- 32 -
Successful candidates will be required to sign a development
contract in accordance with the draft contract prepared by PALMINDUSTRIE
which is satisfactory to the Bank. The role of PALMINDUSTRIE will be
supply of inputs, purchase of FFB at the farm or mill gate and provision of
technical advice.
4.07      Credit Arrangements.  Credit to smallholders and medium-sized
plantations will be provided through FER (para 3.23). The PER will be
established with a legal status satisfactory to the Bank (para 2.22). An
assurance was obtained at negotiations that smallholders and medium-sized
plantation operators will sign a development contract with PAIMINDUSTRIE
for participation in their respective planting programs (para 7.01(m)).
Terms and conditions of FER's loans to smallholders and medium sized
plantation operations are presented in paras 5.30 and 5.32.
D. Implementation
4.08      The main elements of the project will be implemented over a five
year period as shown in Annex 4-4, the planting program, the technical
assistance and research components being achieved over five years and the
first phase of mill construction (financed by CDC and EIB) being completed
after seven years. Project disbursements are spread fairly evenly over the
first five years and thereafter decline. Procurement documents for land
clearing equipment were prepared during the second semester of 1984 with
delivery during 1985 for clearing areas for 1986 planting. Nursery
facilities for 1986 field planting have been established. Preparation of
procurement documents for oil mills will be initiated in the first semester
of 1988 so that the first mill at Neka can be operational at the end of
1990. Implementation of the proposed research program will be
straightforward as the work is largely a continuation of existing programs.
E. Monitoring and Evaluation
4.09      The management information and cost control systems for
PALMINDUSTRIE's industrial plantations, which are already well established,
will be further refined generally and developed to allow cost comparison
and analysis of man day utilisation in accordance with the agreed
time-table for the Action Program (para 2.15). The Ministry of Agriculture
will have responsibility for monitoring PALMINDUSTRIE's performance through
the two project-supported visiting advisers (3 months/year) (para 3.19) and
the independent auditors (para 3.30).
4.10      Monitoring of sualiholder performance will be carried out by the
extension service who will record the progress of smallholder plantings
established under the project in terms of technical standards achieved with
respect to establishment success, cover crop establishment, weeding
standards and standard of pest control measures. Yields of FTB will be
recorded on a per hectare basis for each smallholding. The PALMINDUSTRIE
field inspector will be required to report on the efficiency of monitoring
as carried out by the extension service.



- 33 -
V. PRODUCTION, MARKETS, PRICES AND FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Technical Aspects
5.01      The planting program will include new planting and replanting.
The technique of land clearing to be used will depend upon the nature of
the existing vegetation and the type of operator. With industrial
plantations, forest, bush and old oil palms will be pushed over by
bulldozer and heaped in vindrows, along the contour, where appropriate, and
burnt. In the case of medium-sized estates forest will be felled by chain
saw and then pushed into windrows by bulldozers. Smallholders will clear
by manual means.
5.02      All areas will be planted with selected Dura x Pisifera hybrid
polybagged plants protected from rodents by wire netting guards. The
germinated seed for nursery planting will be purchased from IRHO. Palms
will be field planted at a density of 143 per ha (9 m x 9 m). A leguminous
cover crop will be established and maintenance will be carried out by hand
weeding during the immature period. In replanted areas emphasis will be
placed upon achieving rapid cover crop spread over the felled oil palm 1ogo
to reduce the level of breeding of Oryctes monoceros or coconut beetle, an
Important pest of young palms. On industrial plantations during the mature
period, maintenance will be effected by alternating rounds of manual and
chemical weedIng.
5.03      Manuring during immaturity will be based upon the application of
N, K, P and Mg as single fertilizers. Detailed schedules are in Annex 3-1.
After commencement of production, nutrient needs will be identified by leaf
analysis and are likely to be generally restricted to superphosphate in the
west and muriate of potash on the tertiary sands of the centre and east.
5.04      Harvesting will generally be carried out in weekly rounds.
Smallholders would deliver cut bunches to the roadside for collection by
PALMINDUSTRIE trucks prior to processing in the central mill. Harvesting
at the correct stage of ripeness will be emphasized by the extension
service so as to maximise the oil/bunch extraction rate.
B. Project Production
5.05      Yield estimates  are based on PALMINDUSTRIE's own recorded
experience over their total planted area as well as on comprehensive
analyses by IRHO. During the three years 1981-83 the mean yield per
hectare per annum was 10 tons. Industrial plantation yields havt been
assumed to be at about historical levels and yield profiles are summarized
In the graph below. The yield level projected is closely related to the
average moisture deficit of an area. Production is projected to commence
in the fourth year after field planting. Smallholder yields are generally
estimated at 70Z of the adjacent industrial plantation, medium-sized
plantation yields at 90%. Oil extraction rates are assumed to be 20.7% on
average from replantings of industrial plantations and smallholder
plantings and 22.5% for new industrial and medium-sized plantations. The
kernel extraction rate is assumed to be 4.5X. Details of the basis for
yield projections are in Annex 5-1.

C. Markets and Prices
The Domestic and Export Market for Edible Oils and Crude Palm Oil
5.07      The world and Ivorian market for palm oil as well as
international and domestic prices are discussed in Annex 5-3 and briefly
summarized below. The graph below summarizes the domestic market situation
for edible oils. It shows domestic demand for edible oils of industrial
origin and the production of crude edible oils expressed in equivalent
refined, edible oil terms. In 1985, total production of vegetable oil in
terms of edible oil is expected to amount to 109,000 mt (all time high
1980: 138,000 mt) which would exceed domestic demand for edible oil by
26,000 mt. Apparent per capita consumption in Ivory Coast of traditional
and industrial vegetable oils was in 1982, 13.6 kg/year (Cameroon 15.4
kg/year; Nigeria 13.0 kg/year; Ghana 6.2 kg/year). Assuming unchanged per
capita consumption of industrial edible oils, the domestic demand for
edible oils is projected to exceed domestic supply without the project by
1993.

The Pricing Reform
5.08      The objective of the pricing reform In the oil palm sub-sector Is
to ensure the payment to PAKIXNDUSTRIE and outgrovers of the full world
market equivalent value of their produce, adjusted to reflect the
opportunity value of foreig exchange and the effects of tariffs on
production costs. This would mean gradually progrossilg towards a net
effective protection coefficient of unity.
5.09      The Price of Refined Palm Oil.  The Governmnt has agreed, under
the structural adjustment program supported by the Bank, to Implement a
reform of Industrial incentives implying tho harmonization of tariff
protection across the various sub-sectors. Within this framework, the
Government has been *Aked to present a plan for the implementation of the
tariff reform in the local vegetable oil processing sector. This plan will
include the elminiatiLon of all quantitative restrictions on Imports and
exports at all levels of the palm oil processing chain and the deregulation
of palm oil consumer prices (para 5.12).
5.10      The Price of Crude Oil Palm Products.  When CSSPPA was in charge
of the marketing of the crude palm oll, the domestic price was largely
equal to the export equivalent spot price. PALMINDUSTRIE still exports



- 37 -
part of Its production at pravailing world market prices, but since
PALXINDUSTRIS took over the marketing of the crude palm oil products from
CBSPPA, the transfor price of crude palm oil to the doms tic refining
Industry for domestic consumption purposes ham been determined on the basis
of an indicativa formula. Tha forzuula links the domestic price with
long-term world market price trends, but also Includes domsatic price
developments as a major component. Since the Introduction of tho
Indicative formula domestic prices have in general been below export
equivalent prices, sometimes as low am 401 below. According to the
proposed pricing arrangements, the current price fixing formula will be
eliminated (Annex 5-4). Imports and exports of crude palm oil will be
totally free. The transfer pries of crude palm oil from PALMNDUSTRIS to
the domestic refining Industry will be determined by the two concerned
parties through cormercial contracts freely negotiated.  This should  esoult
In a transfer price negotiated at a level between the Import and xport
parity price of crude palm oll. The Govermnmet has agreed to the pricing
arrangement proesnted above. Its Implementation has already started end
Its various components are spolt out In a supplmntal letter to the Loan
Agreoment.

bunches have been revised at long intervals and uneven rates as shown by

5.13      The net value of the PFB will be the basis of a producer price
structuro with several steps, including a base price and productivity
promium.  A base price will be paid to outgroweru below a certain minimum
yleld of PFB per hectare (5 tons/ha/year). The premium, paid at regular
Intervals, will be paid increasing with sales to PALMINDUSTRIE (Annex 5-5).
This price structure will provide the stimulus to production, encourage
intensification, and facilitate price adjustments and price stabilization.
The same farmgate price will be paid to both smallholders and medium-sized
plantations. However, since medium-sized plantations will service their
debt individually and PALMINDUSTRIE's services to the medium-sized
plantations will be substantially less than to the smailholders, the
medium-sized plantations will receive periodical compensation.

5 15      It is essential that the proposed pricing reform be Implemented
in unison at all levels of the processing chain to avoid that elthar
outgrowers, PALMINDUSTRIE or the refining industry be hurt due to price
increases upstream. The transition from the present to the proposed
pricing system will be carried out in two phases. In the first phase, the
price of FFB and crude palm oil will be directly linked to world market
prices as indicated in p4ras 5.10 to 5.12 above. In the second phase, a
tariff-cum-subsidy schame will be gradually introduced in the palm oil
sector to provide outgrowers, PALMINDUSTRIE and the domestic refining
industry with a net effective protection equal to unity. Implementation of
the first phase of the pricing reform will be carried out under the
project. Implementation of the second phasa vill be an integral part of
the proposed SAL III conditionality, to be nagotiatad by the end of 1985.
It will be carried out, during the project period, in full synchronleation
with the changes in incentives for other crops to ensure consistency within
the agricultural sector.
D. Financial Analysis of PALMINDUSTRIE
5.16      The principal assumptions underlying the economic and flnancial
analysis of PALMINDUSTRIE ara shown In Annex 5-6. The company's projected
consolidated financial statements, are found in Annexes 5-7 and 5-8.
5.17      Capital Structure.  The present financial structure of
PALMINDUSTRIE is sound. Current assets compara favorably with short term
liabilities at a ratio of 2.3:1. The balance sheot for September 30, 1954,
shows long term debt at CFAF 25.9 billion with equity at CPA? 35.5 billion,
a debt-equity ratio of 40/60. At negotiations an assurance was obtained
that PAIMINDUSTRIE will seek the Bankts agreement befora Incurring any new
medium or long term debt which would raise the ratio of debt to equity above
60/40 or would increase its annual debt service to more than half its
projected annual revenues and that no dividends will be paid that reduced
the working capital ratio below 1.5:1 (para 7.01(n)). Professional valuers
reported in June 1984 on the value of PALMINDUSTRIE at September 30, 1983.
Based on a valuation of assets and liabilities, not assets, or equity, are
estlmated at CFAP 96 billion. An alternativa valuation based on projected
earnings set the value of PALMINDUSTRIE at CPAF 76 billion.  The
methodology and assumptions of these evaluations conform with comon
practice, and they set the range for the approxlmate value of PALMINDUSTRIZ
as a going concern.
5.18      Profit Structure.  Oil palm provides about 8OS and coconut 20X of
PALMINDUSTRIE's net contribution. This distribution will change In favor
of oil palm when the production of the now plantings start as no expansion
of coconut production is envisaged in the project period although a coconut
expansion project would be prepared. Provision has been made for the
maintenance of the existing coconut plantations.

5.21      This sensitivity to prices underscores the importance of the
implementation of the pricing reform for the financial profitability of
PALMNIDUSTRIE and for the prospects of attracting private capital to the
further expansion of the oil palm sector. In addition, the combined
sensitivity to prices and agricultural uncertainty calls for prudence in
the estimation of PALMIUDUSTRIE's fund generating capacity to finance its
proposed development plan. In view of the uncertain environment In which
PALMINDUSTRIE operates, the latter should maintain a conservative
debt-equity ratio policy. In view of the company's currently low
debt-equity ratio, the propsed external financing of ClAP 20.0 billion
MOS$40.9 million) will be sufficient to allow PALMINDUSTRIE to finance its
proposed development program and sufficiently low to avoid excessive
leverage. It will allow PALMINDUSTRIE to face potential adverse
agricultural (yields) or financial (prices) conditions such as those
depicted in Scenarios II and III above.
5.22      Financial Policy. The proposed financing plan Intends to ensure
that strict technical and financill managemet policies be mantained and
reinforced, that funds for the Implementation of the Project will be
available throughout the project execution period, and that an appropriate
policy for allocation of free funds bo pursued. It in one of the main
purposes of the "Contrat Plan" (para 4.02) to provide the framework for
control of the potentially substantial free funds remaining after meeting
the project investment costs. The "Contrat Plan" will specify the
allocation of funds to ensure replanting and expansion of the surface
planted with oil palm beyond the project, and allocation of the free
funds, if any. The "Contrat Plan" will also provide for Government
financing of any finacing gap that might occur during the project
Implementation period due to any short-fall in PALMINDUSTRIE's
self-financing capacity not covered by the proposed external financing.
5.23      Assuming that 10% of not profits after tax and after allocation
to statutory reserves be allocated to a further reserve, incrasing to 40S
In 1992, free funds amounting to a total of CFAP 56.6 billion would be
available in the period 1986-90. Subsequently, the amounts avallable
Increase sharply. These free funds should in priority be directed to
Investment in the epansion and diversification of tree crops by
PALMINDUSTRIE or an affiliated institution, or they could be disbursed as
dividends to Govenment. In the base case (scenario I), cash flow
projections show net cumulative cash after allocation to free funds rising
from CFAF 8.9 billion in 1985, to CFAF 20.8 billion in 1987 decreasing to
ClAP 11.7 billion in 1989. The negative effect of adversb agricultural
and market conditions on PALMINDUSTRIE's fund gonerating capacity would be



- 42 -
attenuated by a reduction in corporate income tax and could be further
offset by reductions in the substantial allocations to free funds. Given
the proposed external financing, the financing of PALMINDUSTRIE's share of
the proposed project as well as the allocations to free funds indicated
above would be assured even in the low case scenarios.
E. Financial Analysis of FER
5.24      PER will be funded from four sources: (i) a fixed cess of CFAF
1.5 per kg (1985) paid by all producers (industrial plantations,
smallholders and medium-sized plantation holders) deducted by
PALMThDUSTRIE from the purchase price of the fruit at the farm gate or oil
mill; (ii) external funds onlent to PER by the Government; (iii) a
variable cess on FFB harvested by sallholders which would be
progressively Increased during the project so as to cover their debt
service to PER and increase the Fund's reserves; and (iv) repayments of
their loans by owners of medium-sized plantations at a rate which would
also allow the constitution of reserves. The coconut sector does not at
present contribute to the funding of FER as no immediate expansion program
is In view. Details of the FER forecast cash flow are in Annex 5-9.
5.25      FER will channel external funds to smallholders ard medium-sized
plantations. In addition, PER's current own funds from the proceeds of
the replantiug cess (less the funds needed for the financing of the
completion of the EDP project (para 3.08)) will contribute to the
financing of the smallholder and medium-sized plantations in rhe amount of
CFAF 5.2 billion (US$10.6 million).  FER's contribution to the financing
is calculated under the assumption that the rep.aunting cess be revised
annually. A shortfall in production of FFB affectIng FER's
fund-generating capacity is assumed to be met by an increase in the
replanting cess.
5.26      The use of FER as the major instrument of channeling funds to
the oil palm outgrower sector requires that the future status of FER
(para 2.22) provides for the possibility of PER building up reserves to
meet upcoming financing needs. Under the current status, any net cash
surplus must be surrendered to CSSPPA. The adoption of adequate statutes
Is a condition of effectiveness of the proposed project (para 7.02(a)).
PALMINDUSTRIE will be in charge, through a management contract with FER,
of disbursing PER loans, supervising plantation establishment and
recovering FER loans. The signing of a management contract between
PALMINDUSTRIE and PER satisfactory to the Bank will be a condition of loan
effectiveness (para 7.02(f)).

5.28      The smallholder will provide the total cost of labor and small
tools for the creation of the plantations. The value of his inputs
accounts for 52% of the total development cost. PALKMINDUSTRIE will
provide the development Inputs at full cost: seedlings, wire netting,
cover crop seeds, fertilizer, and insecticidet, financed through a loan
from PER. At farm level, assuming a planting program of two ha per year
for three consecutive yer., the uAallholder would obtain a positive
return per manday as from year four of th. planting program. The return
per manday in years 4 and 10 would be CFAP 2,000 and ClAP 7,700
respectively. For comparison, the returns per manday for traditional
coffee and cocoa in full bearing is CFAP 2,816 and CFAP 3,340
respectively.
5.29      Smallholder Debt Recovary.  Smallholder debt is currently
recovered by a ces an the FIB production collected by PALMINDUSTRIE based
on the collective debt of .mallholders. The system vas Introduced in 1984
in ortdr to simplify previous unsatisfactory systems based on standard
credit principles, i.e. individualized debt paid by fixed annuities until
extinction. The main advantage of the cess system is its operational
simplicity, vhich more than compensates for the disadvantages of (i)
havinS the good producer contribute more to the collective debt than the
poor producer; (ii) the smallholder's reduced awareness of his individual
debt burden and net worth; (iii) the debt recovery depending on the
smallholder surrandering his production to PALMINDUSTRIE and not selling
to the secondary market.
5.30      Current Interest rates for preferential activities in the Ivory
Coast, including agriculture, range between 9-11 p.a. In addition, a
service tax of 25% is normally added. Under this project the combined



- 44 -
fixed and variable cess for the smallholder will be equivalent to a loan
to be repaid over the life of the plantation at an interest rate of IlZ
p.a which is equivalent to the estimated opportunity cost of capital to
the economy (11.5Z p.a.). The development loan covers the cost of Inputs,
extension cost and access roads. The difference in the two rates 11% and
5%, the estimated average cost of PER's resources, would allow the fund to
build up reserves to an appropriate level. The cost recovery index
discounted at 11.5%, the opportunity cost of capital, is 0.94 with full
charging for exten:-ion and access road costs.

5.32      As the medium-sized plantations are be expected to constitute
independent enterprises, the investment budget comprises housing for
employees as well as vehicles for collection and transport to the mill of
the FFB harvest. The rate of return shown in the table above assumes that
the medium-sized plantation would be paid the same price as the
smAllholder with no compensation for any differences in the service
extended to smallholders and the medium-sized plantation in question.
However, the expected higher yields obtained by medium-sized plantations
would allow them to benefit from the scaled price structure. The loan to
medium sized plantations owners will be an individualised loan with a rate
of interest of 11% on the basis of repayment over 20 years including 6
years grace.

VI. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A. Project Benefits and Economic Analysis
6.01      The project's quantifiable benefits would be the crude palm and
palm kernel oil extracted from the FFB produced over the life of the palms
on the 64,700 ha of the project. This production would have a cumulative
world market value of about US$1,500 million at constant 1985 export
parity prices. The net cumulative foreign exchange earnings (export
revenue less cost of imported inputs) in constant 1985 terms amount to
US$200 million, while foreign exchange earnings/savings after external
debt service and assuming import substitution for local consumption total
US$212 million (constant 1985). Other benefits are the contribution of
the project to the provision of raw materials to the local fats and oil
industry and the increased diversification of the agricultural sector.
The project would generate 55,000 man years of incremental employment of
which 40,000 man years outside the industrial plantations.
6.02      Economic costs include all investments, maintenance, operating
and overhead costs associated with the plantation establishment (including
access road development and maintenance) as well as harvesting and
processing after plantation maturity. The value of the foregone
production due to the felling of ageing but still producing trees under
the replanting program has been included. An opportunity cost of land has
not been included as the areas to be planted with oil palm are generally
covered with degraded bush with limited commercial value. Labor would be
drawn from local markets, which are competitive, and in which wages
adequately represent the economic cost of labor. Throughout the analysis
a shadow exchange rate of US$1 - CFAF 588 has been used. The principal
assumptions of the economic and financial analysis are in Annex 5-6 and
the project cash flow and economic and financial rates of return are in
Annex 6.
6.03      The economic and financial rates of return under these assump-
tions are summarized in the table below. The economic rates of return
exceed the opportunity cost of capital in Ivory Coast, currently estimated
to be 11.5%. The net present value per ha discounted at the opportunity
cost of capital is also shown in the table and illustrated graphically for
the project as a whole overleaf.

The differences between the ERRs of the three components of the
project as a whole are explained mainly by differences in establishment
costs due to methods of land-clearing and the density and age of the
vegetation in the designated areas: medium-sized plantations are
established in areas with no previous oil palm plantings, vhereas the
smallholder project component to a large extent comprises planting in
areas with degraded vegetation. The wide difference between the



- 47 -
industrial plantations component and the outgrover components is due to
the additional cost of the former arising from the foregone production
from felled oil palm (para 5.06). Economic and financial returns of the
three components at a new nucleus estate show a smaller difference between
the three components. Investment costs at this new site include the
establishment of oil mill and other basic infrastructure, and the data
demonstrate that the economic and financial viability of new oil palm
plantations does not depend on a large amount of sunk costs of investment
in Infrastructure.
B. Project Risks
6.04      The principal sources of project risk are:
Agricultural risks        -    oil palm yields; depend on climatic and
soil conditions, the use of fertilizer,
insect attacks, harvesting organization and
processing efficiency;
-    smallholder interest and response, affects
potentially the project development period.
Financial risks          -    world palm oil prices;
-    wage rates, labor availability;
-    management and control of operating and
overhead costs.
The potential effects of these elements of risk have been
quantified in a sensitivity analysis which is summarized graphically
overleaf . Each graph has on the X-axis the percentage change of the
appraisal value of the parameter and on the Y-axis the resulting economic
rate of return. The switching values at 11.5% are as follows:

6.05      The project is clearly sensitive to variations in benefits, i.e.
FFB yields or world price of oll palm products. In particular, failure to
introduce the proposed tariff on crude palm oil would reduce the economic
return of the project to 14.1%. The graph below illustrates the project's
sensitivity to the combination of decreases in bensfits and increases in
costs. The line in the graph is drawn between pairs of values of cost
increases and bmnefit decreases compared to appraisal values that combined
would reduee the ERR of the Project to 14.8X.

COTS. X OF APPRPASAL ALLE
6.06      The project's economic viability is relatively insensitive to
delays in implementation. Except for the research and technical
assistance component, investment expenditure and project operating costs
follow the implementation of the planting program so that delays in the
program delay project expenditure. The cost of delay in implementation is
limited to the foregone return for the duration of the delay of a
profitable investment. As to the coverage of domestic demand for edible
oils, Ivory Coast would remain a net exporter of vegetable oils through
year 2000 despite a delay in the entire program of up to two years.
C. Environmental Considerations
6.07      Ageing oil palms, bush and logged forest would be replaced with
oil palms which provide satisfactory soil protection due to their crown
formation and the use of cut fronds as mulch, as practised on
PAIMINDUSTRIE plantations. In addition, leguminous cover crops would be
sown to build up soil fertility and where necessary in a few areas,
contour bunds would be established to control erosion. Intercropping on
smallholdings would be restricted to flat and gently undulating land.
With regard to effluent, alternative treatment systems would be studied
and appropriate systems established (para 3.14).



- 51 -
VII. ASSURANCES AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01   Assurances obtained at Negotiations were that:
(a) PALMINDUSTRIE will continue the present arrangements for
employment of personnel from an oll palm management company and
an oil palm research institution during the implementation
period unless PALMINDUSTRIE and the Bank agree otherwise (para
2.12).
(b) Government will consult with the Bank on substantial changes in
PALMINDUSTRIE's organizational structure and will ensure that
the quality of its senior management will be maintained (para
2.12).
(c) PAIMINDUSTRIE will consult with the Bank on the proposed
arrangements for participation by any private investor In the
equity of PALMINDUSTRIE (para 2.13).
(d) Government will undertake to inform the Bank of its proposals
for the future legal and corporate status of PALMINDUSTRIE
(para 2.13).
{e) PALMINDUSTRIE will implement the Action Program in accordance
with the agreed timetable (para 2.15).
(f) Government will maintain the existing San Pedro-Neka road in
a condition which will allow year round transport of the produce
of Blidouba and Neka (para 3.03).
(g) PALMINDUSTRIE will Install during the project period effluent
treatment systems at the new and existing mills according to a
timetable to be presented to the Bank for agreement before
December 31, 1986 (para 3.14).
(h) PALMINDUSTRIE will obtain Bank approval prior to appointment of
the specialists for short term technical assistance and will
consult with the Bank before introducing modifications into its
medium term technical assistance contracts (para 3.19).
(i) PALMINDUSTRIE will submit to its Board of Directors for
approval and to Government and the Bank for information
consolidated annual budgets at least three months before the
start of the financial year (para 3.30).
(j) PALMINDUSTRIE and FER will (i) maintain appropriate records
and accounts; (it) have their accounts audited by independent
auditors acceptable to the Bank; and (iii) submit to the Bank,
audited annual accounts within six months of the end of their
financial year (para 3.30).
(k) PALMINDUSTRIE will prepare a completion report for submission
to the Bank six months after the loan closing date (para 3.30).



- 52 -
(1)  Any substantive change In the "Contrat Plan" will require the
Bank's prior agreement (para 4.02).
(m) Smallholders and medium-sized plantation operators vill sign a
development contract with PALMINDUSTRIE for participation In
their respective planting programs (para 4.07).
(n) PALMINDUSTRIE will obtain the Bank's agreement before Incurring
any new medium or long term debt which would raise the ratio of
debt to equity above 60/40 or which would increase Its annual
debt service to more than half its projected annual revenues and
that no dividends will be paid that reduced the working capital
ratio below 1.5:1 (para 5.17).
7.02   Conditions of Effectiveness would be:
(a) that the PER will have been established under new statutes
satisfactory to the Bank (para 2.22);
(b) the fulfillment of all conditions of effectiveness of the CCCE,
CDC, and EIB loans (para 3.22);
Cc) the conclusion of subsidiary loan agreements satisfactory to the
Bank between Government and PAIMINDUSTRIE and Government and PER
(para 3.23);
(d) the opening of the Special Account (para 3.29);
(e)  that Government and PALMINDUSTRIE have signed a "Contrat Plan"
on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para 4.02).
(f)  that PALMIDUSTRIE and PER have entered Into a management
contract satisfactory to the Bank (para 5.26).
7.03 A Condition of Disbursement would be:
that PALMINDUSTRIE had signed a contract satisfactory to the
Bank for agricultural research (para 3.16).
7.04      With the above assurances and conditious the project vould be
suitable for a loan of US$13.4 million to Government of Ivory Coast on the
Bank's standard terms.

IVORY COAST
PIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPME   PROJECT
TECHNICAL ASPECTS
1. The ProJect Area
1.1.      The project area stretches from the Liberian border in the west
to the frontier with Ghana in the east. The project would be located in
the tropical forest zone of the southern Ivory Coast at a latitude around
69 North.
2. Climate
2.1.      Rainfall and water deficit.  The monthly rainfall distribution
over the period 1979-84, is given for the main planting sites in Annex 5-1,
page 5. Generally rainfall is concentrated in two wet seasons - March to
June and August/September to October/November. Availability of soil
moisture is best expressed as annual water deficit and is given in Annex
5-1, page 3 for the period 1978-81. Apart from the year 1983, when severe
drought conditions were found all over West Africa the mean annual water
deficlt is of the order of 350 mm which is comparable to that found at the
IRBO La Mg Oil Palm Research Station. At a deficit of this level the mean
yield potential in the mature peak yield period would be some 12-13 tons
ffb/ha. In the south-west and south-east, the deficit is much lower (less
than 100 mn in the former area and 200 mm in the latter) and such
conditions are very suitable for oil palm development.
2.2.      Sunshine and temperature.  The mean annual sunshine hours varies
from 1700 to 1900 in the project area. The monthly average of daily
minimum temperatures is above 18'C which is considered the threshold.
3. Topozraphv and Draiza.Ae
3.1.      Generally the project area has a gently rolling topography.  At
certain locations such as Boubo there are substantial low lying areas
(bas-fonds) with a water table at 1-2 m from the surface depending on the
season. Yields in these areas are higher than would be expected in
relation to the water deficit. In the new areas of the south west the land
surface shows a pattern of finger like parcels of rolling topography
interspersed with low lying areas which should be planted wherever the soil
texture is satisfactory and effective drainage can be installed. The
Tamabo Nord area is a riverine alluvium and represents a new departure in
land utilisation for PALINDlUSTRIE. Such land has a higb yield potential
provided that appropriate drainage (wet season) and water control (dry
season) measures are taken.



- 60 -
Annex 3-1
Page 2 of 6
4. Soils I/
4.1.      The majority of the project area is occupied by upland soils
generally classified as "Sols Ferrallitiques Fortement Dessatures". These
are either soils of the "socle", derived from gneisses, schists or
granites in the areas to the west of Boubo or tertiary sands at Boubo and
the areas to the east. The texture of these soils varies from loamy sand,
sandy loam, sandy clay loam to clay loam. The lowland or hydromorphic
soils vary in texture from sandy clay loam to silty clay (Tamabo Nord).
4.2.      Soils which would be excluded from planting either contain
excessive quantities of lateritic gravel, have shallow hard pans or
suffer from waterlogging. With regard to gravel, the criterion which is
practised is 1.10 m free of gravel concretions.
5. Vegetation
5.1.      In about 33% of the project area, old palms will be replanted.
In a further 33Z, where densification of smallholder plantings will be
carried out, the vegetation is a mixture of light forest, forest
regrowth, perennial crops and annual crops. In the remaining area forest
which has been logged would be converted to oil palms.
6. Oil Palm Technology
6.1.      General. There is a wealth of readily available technical data
in the Ivory Coast arising from the work of the long established IRlO and
the 20 years of experience of PALMINDUSTRIE. The ITHO are technical
advisors to PALMINDUTRIE and publish a regular technical bulletin,
0leagineux. PALMINDUSTRIE are in process of updating their planting
manual which is the standard guide to planting techniques. Technical
information is diffused through inspection visits, extension agents
(smAllholders) and training courses.
6.2.      Technical Issues. The main technical issues which will arise in
the project relate to land clearing techniques, drainage and water control
of low lying areas, the leaf miner, Coelaenomenodera Elaeidis and Fusarium
wilt disease. In the lowlying areas of Tamabo Nord with heavy clay soils,
the use of heavy tractors for land clearing would be avoided. Instead,
trees would be felled wlth chain saws. After burning, felled timber would
be pruned, stacked and re-burned by manual means. Timber would be cleared
from planting points and harvesters paths as necessary by chain saW
operators supported by laborers. The drainage system would be carefully
constructed and operated, so as to maintain adequate drainage in the wet
season and allow the water-table to be kept at an appropriate level in the
I/ Detailed specifications and soil maps are available in the project
working paper Al.l.



- 61 -
Annex 3-1
Page 3 of 6
dry season. The leaf miner would be monitored and controlled using the
existing systems of population census and chemical control. Fusarium wilt
is not a widespread problem in Ivory Coast. There is some evidence however,
that the incidence of the disease is likely to increase in the second
generation of plantings. It is intended therefore, to plant as much
Fusarium tolerant planting material as possible (about 1750 ha per year) in
the areas for replanting, during the project period.
6.3       The planting program would include new planting and replanting.
The technique of land clearing to be used would depend upon the nature of
the existing vegetation and the type of operator. With industrial
plantations, forest, bush and old oil palms would be pushed over by
bulldozer and heaped in windrows, along the contour, where appropriate, and
burnt. In the case of medium sized estates forest would be felled by chain
saw and then pushed into windrows by bulldozers. Smallholders would clear
by manual means.
6.4       All areas would be planted with selected Dura x Pisifera hybrid
polybagged plants protected from rodents by wire netting guards. The
germinated seed for nursery planting would be purchased from IRhO. Palms
would be field planted at a density of 143 per ha (9 m x 9 m). A
leguminous cover crop would be established and maintenance would be carried
out by hand weeding during the immature period. In replanted areas
emphasis would be placed upon achieving rapid cover crop spread over the
felled oil palm logs to reduce the level of breeding of Orvctes monoceros
or coconut beetle, an important pest of young palms. On industrial
plantations during the mature period, maintenance would be effected by
alterlating rounds of manual and chemical weeding.
6.5       Manuring during immaturity would be based upon the application of
N. K, P and Mg as single fertilizers. Detailed schedules are on page 5.
After commencement of production, nutrient needs would be identified by
leaf analysis and are likely to be generally restricted to superphosphate
in the west and muriate of potash on the tertiary sands of the centre and
east.
6.6       Harvesting would generally be carried out in weekly rounds.
Smallholders would deliver cut bunches to the roadside for collection by
PALMINDUSTRIE trucks prior to processing in the central mill. Harvesting
at the correct stage of ripeness would be emphasized by the extension
service so as to maximise the oil/bunch extraction rate.
6.7.      Intercropping of smallholder oil palms would be permitted during
year NO and Ni provided that a minimum distance of 2 metres between oil
palms and intercrops is maintained. In accordance with PALMINDUSTRIE
practice, bananas/plantains would not be accepted as an
intercrop in view of the possible serious competition for light and
nutrients. At the end of the intercrop period, Pueraria cover crop would be
established. Where maize is used as an intercrop the practice is to sno
Pueraria at the same time.



- 62 -
Annex 3-1
Page 4.of 6
6.8.      The Research Copmonent. IRHO has prepared a small research
component to be implemented over a period of three years. This is fully
described in the two documents, "Projet de Renforcement des Recherches
d'Accompagnement a PALMINDUSTRIE, IRHO February 1984" and "Programme de
Recherches, PALMINDUSTRIE, April 1984". The research component would
investigate (i) problems of soil fertility and (ii) Fusarium wilt disease and
was supported by the mission of co-financiers. Under the former
investigation, research would be conducted into the maintenance of soil
fertility in replanted oil palms - tecbniques of land preparation, soil
fertility improvement through use of different cover crops, study of palm
nutrition and the economy of water use. In the latter investigation,
research into Fusarium would be intensified using the latest
technological advances principally, (i) the recently developed ORSTOM
test, which allows the identification of planting materials with bigh
tolerance (902) to Fusarium, compared with the existing tests which
guarantee 60Z tolerance and (ii) the cloning of palms derived from
crosses demonstrating tolerance.
WAPA A
November 1984

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
DETAILED PLANTING PROGRAM
1. Principles in determination of the size of the proLram
1.1.      The major principles taken into consideration were :
- the replanting strategy as related to palm height, age and
yield,
- the domestic demand for palm oil,
- the availability of experienced staff for implementation,
- the success of ongoing planting, development and exploitation
programs,
- the smallholder demand for oil palm planting
- the availability of suitable land
- the availability of project infrastructure such as the
existing EAI facilities, processing capacity and road
networks.
1.2.      The replanting strategy is fully explained in the PALMINDUSTRIE
Preparation Report of April 1984. Major emphasis was placed upon the need
to even out progressively, over time the age composition of plantings, so
as to provide financial stability for future operations.
1.3.      Generally the Agro-Industrial Complexes are adequately staffed
under a classical plantation organisatioD structure. Additional staff
needs for the project are marginal in relation to overall operations and
mainly concern (i) the new plantings for the industrial plantations and
(ii) extensions (largely new plantings) within existing smallholder oil
palm plantings. The latter need will be dealt with through improved
organisation of extension (Annex 4-2). Where estate replanting takes
place there are no additional staff requirements.
1.4.      The recent 1983-85 FED programs wbich will amount to 14,000 ha
over the period were visited by the mission and found to be well
established. The original program of 12,000 ha will be exceeded by 2,000
ha.
1.5.      Smallholder demand and interest for oil palms is high in terms
of the applications for 50,000 ha of planting received by PALMINDUSTRIE
and the satisfactory condition of the 1983-84 plantings. In certain areas
such as Ehania and the south west, interest is enhanced by the ageing of
the cocoa smallholdings, the disease problems experienced in these wetter
areas, more suited to palms than cocoa and the relatively poor
performance of cocoa as opposed to oil palms, as perceived by
smallholders during the severe 1983 drought.
1.6.      The detailed planting program is on pages 3 to 6 of this Annex.



- 66 -
Annex 3-2
Page 2 of 6
2. The feasibility of the proaram
2.1.      The total five year project of some 65,000 ha is considerable
in relation to the present planted area of PALMINDUSTRIE (89,800 ha of
oil palms and 27,600 ha of coconuts) l/. The project would however be
implemented at some 12 management units all of which, except the new area
of Neka, are already adequately provided with staff and facilities.
2.2.      Some 50X of the plantings would be concentrated at Ehania and
in the south-west with 15,000 ha at the former and 21,000 ha at the
latter area. An additional senior staff member has been posted to Neka in
the south-west to take charge of land clearing operations.
2.3.      With the exception in para 2.1 above, the project would be
carried out within the existing infrastructure of PALEINDUSTRIE. The
terrain is straightforward without any important constraints to
development, technical problems are well known and the technology for
their resolution well applied (Annex 3-1).
2.4.      The major area of smallholder activity would be in the
plantings within existing oil palm areas. The approximate number of
smallholders involved in the project would be 8,000 compared with the
existing number of 10,000. Some 50Z of the project participants would be
participants from the previous projects, whose performance was judged
satisfactory. They would receive up to 3 ha of palms so as to bring their
total oil palm holdings to 6 ha. New smallholders would receive 6 ha
spread over 3-4 years provided that their performance continued to be
satisfactory. The above approach is suitably gradual and lays stress on
proven performance.
2.5       The recent experience with the FED program has adequately
prepared PALMINDUSTRIE to launch a project of this magnitude.

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
EFFLUENT TREA T
Introduction
1.        PALMINDUSTRIE have carried out considerable research and
development into the disposal of mill effluent. Effluent treatment
measures should be designed for new and existing mills.
Background
2.        PALMINDUSTRIE operate 12 oil mills on the Agro-Industrial
Complexes. Recommendations for pollution control at these mills are in
Working Paper 1.5. Effluent disposal facilities would be installed at
the mills according to a phased program over a period of 6-8 years. The
first step would be to establish an effluent treatment system at the
existing mill which has the most serious effluent problem. The
experience gained in operation would be used in the design of the system
for the new mills at Blidouba and Neka which will be constructed in 1988.
WAPA A
July 1985

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PREPARATION OF A STRATEGY FOR THE OIL PALM AND COCONUT SUB-SECTOR
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
Introduction
1.        In preparation for the Third Oil Palm Plan, the Government of
Ivory Coast wishes to develop with the assistance of PALMINDUSTRIE, a
strategy for the oil palm and coconut sub-sector.
Background
2.        About  60X of the land area of Ivory Coast is classified as
agricultural (200,000 kWn), out of a total of 320,000 km2. However, the
area actually under cultivation at a-y one time is estimated to be only
64,000 km2 or some 20Z of this agricultural area. Smallholder
agriculture predominates, contributing some 95% of total agricultural
production, with industrial plantation agriculture which is largely oil
palm, coconut, and rubber making up the remaining 5%. Cocoa and coffee,
almost entirely planted on smallholdings, occupy some 2.2 million
hectares and together contribute two thirds of agricultural foreign
exchange earnings. The total area planted with the industrial plantation
tree crops is coconut 47,000 ha, oil palm 90,000 ha and rubber 41,000 ha.
The area within the forest zone amounts to 13.6 million ha. On the
assumption that about 20% of this area would be suited to coconut, oil
palm and rubber, there is therefore a large area of some 2 million ha
potentially suitable for their cultivation. This includes some 800,000
ha of "bas fonds" and up to 100,000 ha of alluvial soils with high water
tables with high potential for smallholder oil palm and coconut
cultivation. Land suitability therefore should not be a constraint.
3.        The oil palm sub-sector largely comprises PALMINDUSTRIE with
some 50,000 ha of industrial plantations and 30,000 ha of timallholdings.
Under the proposed Second Oil Palm Plan, to be implemented during
1985-1990, these areas would be increased to 62,000 and 54,000 ha
respectively. Other oil palm industrial plantations include BLOHORN with
3,500 ha and IRRO with 4,000 ha. In addition there are a few hundred
hectares of oil palm smallholdings planted by individuals outwith
organised programs. The coconut sub-sector consists mainly of
PALMINDUSTRIE with 19,000 ha of industrial plantations and 9,000 ha of
smallholdings. In addition, some 20,000 ha of coconut smallholdings -were
established prior to the establishment of PALMINDUSTRIE. So far
expansion has depended almost entirely on Goveroment intervention in the
sub-sector.



- 74 -                    Annex 3-4
Page 2 of 4
4.        PALMINDUSTRIE represents a major investment by Government in
oil palm and coconut infrastructure, housing and social facilities and in
the training of staff, labor and smallholders. Plantations are under
efficient management, and the financial position of the company is sound.
Smallholder performance has recently Improved following price increases
for fresh fruit bunches. The immediate demand for smallholder
participation in the Second Oil Palm Plan amounts to some 50,000 ha and
further demand is likely to remain strong in view of the attractiveness
of the crop for both cash and domestic consumption purposes.
5.        It was agreed by Government and the co-donors at negotiations
that a strategy and investment plan for the development of the sub-sector
would be prepared during the project peried by the planning staff of
Government and PALMINDUSTRIE. The strategy would be required to be
consistent with Government's overall strategy for the entire tree crop
sector including cocoa, coffee, coconut, oil palm and rubber.
Strategy for Oil Palm and Coconut
6.        The objectives of the study for development of the strategy
would be to:
Xi)    determine the location of soils with high production potential;
(ii)   briefly review the performance and prospects of the oil palm
and coconut sub-sector, given world and domestic market
conditions;
(iii) examine the economic and financial feasibility of oil palm and
coconut production at different levels of management and in a
range of agro-climatic conditions;
(iv)   determine the constraints in the development of the sub-sector,
in particular those relating to availability of capital and
management;
(v)    recommend measures for alleviation of these constraints.
(vi)   draw up an investment plan with realistic targets for
investment.
Detailed Requirements of the StIAX
7.        The study would be carried out in two parts.



- 75 -
Annex 3-4
Page 3 of 4
Part I
8.        The requirements of Part I would be the following:
(i)    briefly review the different units of agricultural production
in the sub-sector and evaluate their performance;
(ii)   on the basis of existing land resource and climatic data,
determine the location, suitability and area (ha) of soils with
a high oil palm and coconut yield potential; if necessary, draw
up terms of reference for further studies that may be required
to support any recommendations;
(iii)  assess the economic and financial potential of further oil palm
and coconut development at different levels of management and
technology and in an appropriate range of agro-climatic
conditions, taking into account the agronomic potential in para
8 (ii) above, as well as existing and planned processing and
marketing facilities and infrastructure;
(iv) estimate export market prospects and market needs, taking into
consideration comparative advantage and options for meeting
domestic vegetable oil needs through importation and
substitution between palm oil, coconut oil and other vegetable
oils;
(v)  determine the major constraints to production and investment in
the sub-sector;
(vi) propose a national long term planting and replanting program
for oil palm and coconut on the basis of economic potential and
Government objectives for the tree crop sector;
(vii) examine the organisational and financing arrangements of
PALMINDUSTRIE and FER, the main agencies for oil palm
development, and assess the suitability of these arrangements
as vehicles for further development of the sub-sector.
Consideration should be given to measures which would optimise:
(a) the returns to investment in PALMINDUSTRIE (to (i) include
the advantages/disadvantages of privatisation and/or splitting
PALNINDUSTRIE into smaller entities, and (ii)the possibility of
PALMINDUSTRIE developing other crops such as rubber within the
sphere of influence of existing plantation Infrastructure); (b)
the involvement of the private sector (smallholder, medium
sized and large scale plantations).
(viii) draw up a strategy and investment plan for the phased
development of the sub-sector over the period 1990-2000 to
include physical areas for development, organisational
arrangements and financing requirements.



- 76 -                     Annex 3-4
Page 4 of 4
Part II
9.        The requirements of Part II would be to carry out detailed
feasibility studies and draw up investment plans for particular sites,
selected by Government and PALMINDUSTRIE following the work of
PALMINDUSTRIE under para 8 (ii). The feasibility study would include
survey of physical resources, definition of land tenure status and
formulation of a project for tree crop development. Detailed terms of
reference would be drawn up nearer the date.
WAPA A
July 1985

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ESTABLISHMENT OF TRAINING PROGRAMS
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
Introduction
1.        PALMIND1USTRIE intends to greatly increase the emphasis on
training, to expand its scope and to organise it on a systematic basis.
The services of a training consultant are required to establish the
proposed training programs.
Background
2.        PALMINDUSTRIE employ some 14,500 persons of whom 14,000 are
located at the Agro-Industrial complexes and 452 are at headquarters in
Abidjan. The numbers of management staff (Directeurs, Cadres) at
headquarters and on the complexes are 135 and 27 respectively. Staff at
supervisor and overseer level amount to about 1,000 and there is a
similar number of specialised workers.
3.        Training has largely been on the job with tecbmica'. instruction
given by IRRO.  In view of the major development program proposed and the
policy of Ivorianisation which is underway, PALMINDUSTRIE has decided to
employ a consultant during 1985, to  (i)  identify training needs and
draw up an outline training syllabus and (1i) review existing training
facilities and determine the mecessity for further lnhouse facilities.
The Personnel Department is currently preparing job descriptions for all
posts along with an analysis of the skills required. The consultant
would prepare a training program to meet these requiremezits.
4.        During project implementation (1986-1990) a training consultant
would also be required for a total period of 24 man months to train the
trainers and establish the training programs. The consultant would
report directly to the Personnel Manager of PALMINDUSTRIE who is
responsible for implementation of the personnel policies of the company.
5. Detailed Requirements
These would be:
(i)     coordination of the preparation of training programs by
PALMINDUSTRIE senior staff in relation to the training needs
already identified (para 3); these programs would cover
agriculture, processing, administrEtion, finance, commercial
operations and senior management;



- 78 -
Annex 3-5
Page 2 of 2
(ii)    implementation of the training programs to be given lnitially
by (a) senior management staff of PALNINDUSTRIE; (b)
non-PALMINDUSTRIE specialists located in the Ivory Coast; and
(c) eventually largely by PALMINDUSTRIE speciallst trainers;
training of local trainers would be an important element of
implementation;
(iii)   organisation and management of trainers and of training
facilities, budgeting and financial management of training;
(iv)    assistance to the Personnel Manager with the integration of
training into the personnel development process; this will
require systematic evaluation and follow-up of trainees.
WAPA A
January 1985

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
EVALUATION OF PROJECT PROGRESS
AGRICULTURAL AND FINANCIAL ADVISERS
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
Background
1.        PALMINDUSTRIE, a Government-owned Corporation is responsible
for some 50,000 ha industrial plantations and 30,000 ha mullholdings
which were established under the First Oil Palm Plan. A Second Oil Palm
Project is now proposed, to be financed by PALMINDUSTRIE and five
external financiers, which would comprise a project for the planting and
replanting of some 65,000 ha of which about 6OZ would be smullholder
planting. The Government wishes to employ, on a visiting part-time
basis, two Independent advisers, one on agriculture and one on finance
who would report to the Board of Directors of PALINDUSTRIE on the
standard and cost of oil palm operations of PALMINDUSTRIE.
Experience and Rualificutions
I. AGRICULTURAL ADVISER
2.        At least 15 years experience of oil palm plantation mannagement
at an international level and preferably in both Africa and S.E. Asia.
3.        Ability to report concisely and analytically on progress in all
aspects of nursery organisation, land clearing, field planting,
maintenance and harvesting and collection of FFB and to co-ordinate the
technical and financial analysis of plantation operations.
II. FINANCIAL ADVISER
4.        At least 10 years experience of the financial management of a
lirge agricultural enterprise preferably in the plantation sector.
5.        Ability to assess the efficiency of reporting and control
systems In plantations, mills and at head office and to evaluate the
financial performance of PALMINDUSTRIE.



- 82 -
Annex 3-7
Page 2 of 2
Duties
6.        The acvlsers would carry out the following duties for a period
of five years. Two visits would be mbde each year by each adviser 1/.
The Agricultural Adviser would co-ordinate the work of both advisers.
Duties which would include report writing at the end of each visit would
be:
(1)     evaluating work standards being achieved relative to cost
in nurseries, land clearing, field plantlng, maintenance and
harvesting on the basis of inspection of a cross section of
field areas of different ages and of the reports made by the
PALMINDUSTRIE inspectors;
(ii)    comparative analysis of performance of individual
Agro-Industrial Complexes in respect of their oil palm
activities;
(iii)   scrutiny of work programs for the following year;
(iv)    assessment of PALMINDUSTRIE's financial performance;
(V)     assessment of PALMINDUSTRIE's progress in meeting its overall
corporate objectives and in development of an adequate manpower
capacity to meet these objectives.
