IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PROJECT ORGANISATION AND MANAGEMENT
1. Background
1.1.      In 1963, the state corporation, SociEte pour le Diveloppement et
l'Exploitation du Palmier a Huile (SODEPAI) was established by Government
decree, and given responsibility for the implementation of the entire first
oil palm plan (Plan Palmier) and then from 1967, for the coconut plan.
Following a series of corporate restructurings which commenced in 1969,
PALMINDUSTRIE, originally a semi-public entity which owned the oil mills,
was in 1977 converted into a state corporation. PALMINDUSTRIE's
responsibilities have gradually increased and it is now in charge of the
oil palm and coconut industrial plantations, the smallholder oil palm and
coconut program, processing and marketing. The detailed background to the
institutional changes which have accompanied the oil palm and coconut
developments is given in the Operations Evaluation Department (OED) Impact
Evaluation Report 1/.
2. Organisational Structure a.d Staffing
2.1.      The organisation chart of PALMINDUSTRIE is in Annex 4-1.
PALMINDUSTRIE comprises the headquarters at Vridi near the port of AbidJan,
the nine Agro-Industrial Complexes (Ensemble Agro-Industriel) where the
industrial and smallholder oil palm and coconut plantings are located, 12
oil palm mills, an oil seeds mill at Vridi and a palm oil bulking
installation at San Pedro. PALMINDUSTRIE employs some 14,500 people of
which some 14,000 are In the Complexes and 452 at headquarters (page 6 and
7). The numbers of management staff (Directeurs, Cadres) at headquarters
and the Complexes respectively are 135 and 27. At the former location, 110
are local staff and 25 are expatriate. In the field, of the nine Managers
of Complexes, eight are local. These Managers are assisted by a total of
15 expatriates of which 12 are in the mills and three in the field.
2.2.      Expatriates are employed through three separate arrangements :
(i) under a contract with UNIPLANT; (ii) under a contract with IRHO and
(iii) by direct employment. UNIPLANT comprises a consortium of three
international, long established plantation companies, SOCFIN, PLANTATIONS
TERRES ROUGES and SODECI.
2.3.      The staff provided under the above contracts have executive
authority in the following seven positions : (i) UNIPLANT; Technical
1/ Impact Evaluation Report Ivory Coast : Oil Palm and Coconut Development
Projects. OED, May 1984. paras 1.09 - 1.15; paras 2.16 -2.23.



- 92 -
Annex 4-2
Page 2 of 9 pages
Manager, Financial Manager, Personnel Manager, Oil Seed Kill Manager and
(ii) IRRO; Assistant General Manager, Manager of Programming and Studies
and Field Inspector (existing plantings). Under the UNIPLANT contract, it
is intended that the following three additional appointments will be made
in early 1985; Visiting Manager Marketing Department; Central Worshop
Manager and a Reserve Manager for the Agro-Industrial Complexes.
3. Role and Functions of Headquarters
3.1.      PALMINDUSTRIE Headquarters is responsible for (i) policy
formulation with respect to administration, finance and technical
operations (ii) the overall planning and phasing of industrial plantation
and smallholder programs and (iii) the direction and control of the
Agro-Industrial Complexes which are charged with the implementation of
these programs at field level. The headquarters has also the
responsibility for overall personnel and financial management, for
procurement, marketing and for surveys and studies.
3.2.      The effective implementation of field programs ultimately depends
upon the standard of management at field level. To achieve a high level of
efficiency field management must be fully supported by specialists in the
spheres of management, personnel development, production and processing and
there must be a well organised system of inspection, accounting and
reporting. During the past 3-4 years, PALMINDUSTRIE has created a
headquarters structure with a satisfactory capacity for planning,
direction, support and control. The maintenance of this structure during
the envisaged project is essential to the continuing efficiency of
PALMINDUSTRIE.
3.3.      Direction, support and control of field progrAms are provided
through regular visits by the Manager of Agricultural Production (Directeur
Production Agricole) and the PALMINDUSTRIE Production and Processing
Inspectors. Control is also obtained through a system of annual budgets
and monthly progress reports and accounts. This system, which provides the
management information necessary for the running of the company is in the
process of being refined and computerised. In addition, external control
is carried out by inspectors of the Ministry of Agriculture for technical
programs (Cellule Palmier) and by an independent company of external
auditors for financial matters. Finally, under the project, independent
supervision of technical aspects of the proposed program would be carried
out by an Agricultural Adviser and a Financial Adviser.
3.4.      PALMINDUSTRIE is now placing major emphasis on personnel
development; having recently recruited a Personnel Manager through UNIPLANT
for this purpose. A personnel policy is being developed. This includes a
well devised system for staff performance evaluation and for provision of
performance incentives. Programs for career development and manpower
planning and control are being worked out. Training in technical aspects is
provided by IRHO. There is a need however for more comprehensive training
programs to include estate management and administration, estate accounting



- 93 -
Annex 4-2
Page 3 of 9 pages
and smallholder administration. Such training programs would be developed
during 1985 and 1986. The need for additional training facilities would
also be studied. These facilities, if required, would not be financed
under the project, but would be financed separately by PALMINDUSTRIE.
4. Role and Functions of Agro-Industrial Complexes
and Relations with Headquarters.
4.1.      The organisation of a typical Complex is on page 9. The Manager
of a Complex is responsible for ensuring that the headquarters plans for
production and development are implemented In a timely, efficient and
economic manner. It is also his responsibility to assemble all essential
information relating to development and production in the form of reports
and accounts and transmit this information to headquarters for evaluation
and action.
4.2.      The satisfactory operation of a Complex depends upon the
effective delegation of responsibility. The organisation chart of a
PALMINDUSTRIE Complex is designed to meet this objective. The Manager
concentrates upon planning, organisation, co-ordination, general control
and supervision. He also ensures that adequate attention is given to the
training of his subordinate staff and labor. Responsibility for the direct
control of field operations rests with the Plantation Mbanger and his
assistants; each assistant being responsible for an area of about 1,000 ha.
Assistants are supported by supervisors and overseers, the latter in a
ratio of one per 25 laborers.
4.3.      The two main spheres of operation at the level of a Complex in
production are the harvesting and transport of fruit on the one hand and
processing on the other. These are the responsibility of the Plantation
Manager and the Mill Manager respectively. The co-ordination of harvesting
and processing operations being under the close control of the Manager of
the Complex.
4.4.      Since 1983, PALMINDUSTRIE has adopted a gradual policy of
decentralisation, taking into account the management capabilities of
senior staff at field level and where appropriate providing increasing
responsibility to the Managers of the Complexes. The objectives of this
policy are to limit overhead costs and increase autonomy and efficiency at
field level at a time of major expansion of PALMINDUSTRIE. This allocation
of mote responsibility to Managers has been accompanied by strong
headquarters control over physical targets and costs which would be
continued throughout the project period.
4.5.      High standards of industrial plantation require a well trained
and healthy labor force. During the four previous oil palm and coconut
projects, PALMINDUSTRIE has continued to Improve the living conditions of
the labor force on the industrial plantations to a level which is generally
satisfactory. The housing program currently accomnodates 12,000 workers



- 94 -
Annex 4-2
Page 4 of 9 pages
while the project will add 1,000 houses of various types. There are
currently 5 hospital(s) and 50 dispensaries which allow for the treatment
of both plantation workers and smallholder participants. The education
facilities include 50 schools which accommodated 4,000 pupils In 1984.
Finally, PALMINDUSTRIE has also built markets, TV showrooms, soccer fields,
churches and mosques at each complex.
5. PALMINDUSTRIE Extension Services
5.1.      Background.  In addition to oil palm industrial plAntations
PALMINDUSTRIE has a major role In the implementation of the Important
smallholder program. The latter currently represents 40 Z of all oil
palm plantings in the Ivory Coast. The success of the large suallholder
program to he Implemented during the project will be directly related to
the organisation and quality of PALMINDUSTRIE's extension services.
5.2.      Extension services in the oil palm and coconut sub-sector
remained under the responsibility of SODEPALM from 1963 till 1981/82 when
they were transferred to PALMINDUSTRIE. The ratio of extension workers per
area of oil palm had been anticipated, in 1963, to be no less than 1 per
500 ha. However, operations commenced at the much lower rate of one
extension worker per 170 ha, in 1965. It then, rapidly increased to 1 per
300 ha in 1970, 1 per 500 ha in 1974 and 1 per 550 ha In 1977. Meanwhile,
In 1978, the distribution of inputs and the collecting of farueru'ffb were
suddenly passed on from SODEPALM to PALMINDUSTRIE. As a result of this
precipitous transfer of responsibilities, both the extension staff and the
farmers became less motivated. By September 1982. the average ratio of
extension worker par area of oil palm had reached 1 per 650 ha with
extremes of over 1,000 ha in some locations. Thus the total number of
extension agents dropped from 86 to 57 between 1973 and 1982.
5.3.      Because of the lack In both quantity and quality of extension
services, it was recommended in the early 1980's to (I) increase
the number of extension workers in the fitld, significantly (it)
reorganize the extension services and (1i.) Improve the competence of the
extension agents. As a first consequence, there were again some 71
extension agents looking after 35,000 ha of oil palm smallholdings or an
average of 500 ha per agent, in May 1984.
5.4       At headquarters, the smallholder program comes under the
Agricultural Production Department or Direction de la Production Agricole
(DPA). The program Is headed by the Chief of Suallholder Services (Chef
Service Plantations Villageoises) and his deputy. At the field level there
are currently four Regional Coordinators - (Responsables Rfgionaux) - each
acting as advisor to the Manager of a particular Agro-Industrial-Complex on
matters related to smallholders Including extension and the collectlng of
farmers' ffb. The Regional Coordinators are directly answerable to the
DPA.
5.5.      While each industrial plantation is headed by a plantation
manager, there is not yet a corresponding position for similar areas under
smallholdings. Instead the extension agents are supervised by Assistants
in an average ratio of one assistant for 4-5 extension agente.

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
CROP YIELD PROJECTIONS
1. The basis for yield prolection
1.1       There are considerable data on yield behaviour in the Ivory
Coast, from PALMINDUSTRIE itself, where production commenced in 1967 and
from the IRHO Research Station at La Me. Recently, (1978) IRHO carried
out a yield analysis of PALMINDUSTRIE, (SODEPALM, Palmier a guile et
Cocotier: Analyse des Resultats de la Production obtenue).
1.2       Project yield projections are based primarily on the past
yields of a particular Industrial plantation. Yields have been projected
at about historical levels. In new areas yields are based on the
relationship established by IRHO between yield and annual water deficit.
(The annual water deficit, the rainfall data and the yield/water deficit
relationship are in pages 3 to 7 of the Annex.
2. The projections
2.1       Yields of ffb on the industrial plantations are projected
conservatively, (page 8) generally at the same levels as in the past.
During 1981-83 the OED Report assessed the mean industrial plantation
yield at 10 tons/ha per annum. Allowance has not been made for the
likely increase in yield of some 25Z, to be expected from the genetic
improvement of the IRHO planting materials to be used for the project as
compared with the materials produced in the sixties. Yields of new
plantings and replanting are estimated to be at the same level.
2.2       Smallholder yields per hectare have not been recorded
accurately in the past. In the OED Report they were considered to have
reached 9-10 tons ffb/ha in year 10 followed by a gradual fall of 1
ton/ha per year. This poor performance in the later years was related to
poor prices, inadequate extension (in particular the late introduction of
the Malaysian harvesting pole) and poor selection of farmers. Yields of
smallholdings are estimated at 70Z of the adjacent industrial plantation
except in the case of Toumanguie, where present smallholder yields are
exceptionally high and at the other end of the scale, at Bolo/Soubre
where a divergence larger than 30% would be expected on the basis of
poorer past smallholder performance.
2.3       Yields of small/medium enterprises are taken to be 90X of the
industrial plantations.



- 106 -
Annex 5-1
Page 2 of 8
2.4       At the maJor new areas of Blidouba and Neka-Grabo, the extent
of gravelly soil is considered insufficient to warrant yield adjustment
on this account. Furthermore the gravel is fairly loose and well
dispersed.
2.5       In 1984 oil extraction rates were 21% for the mixture of
production from both industrial plantations and smalIholdings. At Iboke
plantation with fruit bunches derived only from the industrial
plantation, the extraction rate was 24%. Conservatively, oil extraction
rates are set at 20.7% for the mixture of industrial plantation and
smallholding production which will be derived from the replanting of
plantations and extensions of smallholdings and at 22.5% for the new
areas which largely comprise industrial plantation and small/medium
enterprises.
2.6       The kernel extraction rate is assumed to be 4.5% which is again
conservative.
WAPA A
November 1984

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
MARKETS, PRICES AND DEMAND
World Market for Pats and Oils
1.        An important characteristics of the World fats and oils economy
is the large number of oil-bearing raw materials. On the basis of their
source, fats and oils can be grouped into vegetable oils and animl fats
(including marine oils). Vegetable oils can be further broken down into
oils from annual oilseeds (such as soybeans. groundnuts and
sunflowerseeds) and those from perennial tree crops (such as coconut oil,
palm oil and palm kernel oil). Oils of the first group are liquid at
room temperature (in temperate climates) and are referred to as "soft"
oils. Oils of the second group, known as "hard" oils8 have a higher
melting point and are usually solid. Hard oils contain a higher
percentage of saturated fatty acids than soft oils.
2.        Demand. Worldwide, the apparent consumption of fats and oils
grew steadily at an average rate of 3.4Z p.a. in the period from 1961 to
1982. In 1982 Industrial countries accounted for just over 332 of world
consusption, down slightly from more than 342 in the early 1960s. During
the early 1960s, developlig countries consumed about 152 of total world
production of fats and oils. By 1982 this share had increased to 56Z of
world consumption. Within the developing region, Asia's share of fats
and oils consumption in 1982 was about 612 of total regional consumption;
the corresponding shares for Africa, Latin America and Southern Europe
were approximately 162, 16X and 6Z respectively.
3.        Regional consumption patterns of fats and oils generally
reflects regional production patterns and natural storage conditions.
Economic protection and transportation costs play an important role in
the preference for locally produced fats and oils. In temperate zones,
soybeans and other annual oilseed crops supply most of the oils needed in
the manufacture of margarine, shortening and other fat products. In
tropical and semi-tropical zones, coconut oil, palm oil and groundnut oil
dominate vegetable oil consumption.
4.        Per capita consumption of fats and oils depends largely on
income, Table 1. Statistical analysis indicates that, at low income
levels, demand for fats and oils increases rapidly vith increasing
incomes. At high income levels - roughly those now reached in the
United States, Canada and some West European countries - demand becomes
much less responsive to increases in income and per capita consumption
levels off at about 30 kg per year.

Annex 5-3

5.        In many industrial countries, therefore, no large increase in
overall demand is likely unless new products and end-uses are developed.
As the potential for expanding consumption declines in industrial
countries, an increasing share of world production of fats and oils have
to be marketed in developing countries where average per capita
consumption is only about one-third of that in industrial countries.
6.        World consumption of vegetable oils is expected to grow at
about 3.2Z p.a. in the period 1985-95, slightly below the historical
trend rate of 3.4Z p.a. during the 1970-82 period. In recent years, the
demand for vegetable oils has grown the fastest in developing countries
and this trend is expected to continue. Vith their expected population
growth rate, higher expected Income growth rate, and higher income
elasticity of demand for vegetable oils, their share of total consumption



- 117 -
Annez 5-3
Page 3 of 12
is expected to increase from 55.8Z in 1982 to 59.6S in 1990 and to 62.5Z
in 1995. The share of total consumption of the industrial countries is
expected to fall from 33.6Z in 1982 to 30.2Z in 1990 and 27.8Z in 1995.
The share of the CPEs is expected to decline marginally, from 10.6Z in
1992 to 10.2Z in 1990 and 9.7% in 1995.
7.        In per capita terms, consumption of vegetable oils in
developing countries is projected to increase from 8.1 kg in 1982 to 9.4
kg in L990 and 10.6 kg-in 1995.
8.        suppl    The supply of fats and oils, as a group, is highly
price-inelastic in the short-run because of the long gestation period and
economic life-span of tree crops   Also, many fats and oils are obtained
as by-products when oil-bearing materials, such as oilseeds. are
processed. The demand for oilseed meal and the demand for fats and oils
which are recovered simultaneously are governed by different market
forces. Thus, the supply of vegetable oils will increase if there is a
strong demand for oilseed meals, irrespective of the prices of vegetable
oils.
9.        Since the-early 19609, world production of vegetable oils.has
grown at an average rate of about 3.4Z. During this period, supplies of
vegetable oil grew faster than those of Aniol fats and marine oils.
Several factors accounted for the increase in vegetable oil supplies.
First, the growing demand for soybean meal led to a sharp increase in
soybean oil supplies. A second factor was the surge in pals oil
supplies. The high yields of palm oil under favorable growing conditions
in Malaysia and some West African countries such as the Ivory Coast made
oil palm one of the most commercially attractive tree crops.
10.       Production of fats and oils is projected to grow at an average
annual rate of 3.21 over the 1985-95 period, slightly below the
historical rate of 3.9% in 1961-82. The United States and Brazil should
continue to dominate soybean production, although a number of other
countries such as Argentine and Paraguay are expected to increase their
market share. Malaysia and Indonesia are expected to increase their
share of world palm oil production.
11.       Trade. Trade in vegetable oils is expected to increase faster
in developing countries because per capita oil consumption is still
relatively low and also because of their higher expected population
growth rate, higher expected income growth rate, and higher income
elasticity of demand for vegetable oils.
12.       Soybean oil and palm oil are likely to continue to dominate
trade in vegetable oils. Both are generally cheaper than other vegetable
oils in the international market and they constitute by far the largest
portion of the ingredients In the manufacture of vanaspati. With the
expected continued large increases in palm oil production, particularly
from Malaysia and Indonesia, the market share of palm oil is expected to
increase even faster than that of soybean oil.



- 118 -
Annex 5-3
Page 4 of 12
13.       Prices.  When the prices of vegetable oils that are important
in world trade are compared on the basis of their average prices, three
distinct group can be delineated: groundnut, cottonseed and lauric oils
(coconut and palm kernel oils) are the highest-priced oils; soybean,
palm, sunfloverseed and rapeseed oils are the medium priced oils; marine
or fish oils are the cheapest available.
14.       Technological improvements in the refining and use of fats and
oils have increased their interchangeability. This has affected prices
in three ways: (a) prices have become more important in the
manufacturer's choice of fats and oils, (b) price differentials among
fats and oils have narrowed; and (c) the increased possibility of
substitution has encouraged the use of cheaper oils, such as soybean oil
and palm oil, at the expense of higher-priced oils.
15.       Barring any major reduction in projected supplies, long-term
real prices of vegetable oils are projected to fall from their current
relatively high levels. Although demand prospects for vegetable oils,
particularly in developing countries, are expected to remain quite
favorable, supplies (especially of soybean and palm oils) are likely to
be more plentiful - leading to a general softening of vegetable oil
prices.
16.       Table 2 below provides recent data on world production and
exports of palm and palm kernel oil. Clearly, Ivory Coast's market share
is minimal and even considerable expansion of exports would be unlikely
to affect world market prices.

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
REFINED AND CRUDE PALM OIL PRICE STRUCTURE
1.        Until the transfer of the marketing of crude palm oil and palm
kernel oil from CSSPPA to PALMINDUSTRIE, crude palm oil was sold to the
local processing industry at world market price with adjustment for cif,
port charges and handling costs. At the time of the transfer of the
marketing responsibilities to PALMINDUSTRIE, an indicative formula was
devised by the Ministry of Industry and Commerce. Whereas
PALMINDUSTRIE's export sales of crude palm oil have been effected at
world prices, the formula has been used in fixing the transfer price of
crude palm oil to the local refining industry. The formula combines the
estimated trend of world palm oil prices in the very long run and local
producer and consumer price developments. The prices fixed by means of
the indicative formula have in the period since its introduction resulted
in distortions between world and domestic transfer prices with the
transfer price being as low as 402 below export equivalent world prices.
2.        It is par; if the structural adjustment program currently being
discussed between the Government and the Bank to ensure a close and
automatic link between world and domestic prices of the major
agricultural commodities. In the context of this pricing reform, the
cofinanciers of the proposed project and the Government have agreed on a
reform of current pricing mechanisms in the oil palm sector along the
lines indicated below:
i)     de facto quantitative restrictions to importing or
exporting crude paLm oil would be removed;
(ii)    the transfer price of crude palm oil between
PALMINDUSTRIE and the domestic refining industry to be
fixed within the framework of freely negotiated
commercia.'.. contracts between the two concerned parties;
(iii) provision of a floor price for smallholders to ensure a



- 128 -
Annex 5-4
Page 2 of 2
minimum return per manday of work and their continued
interest in the crop in case of short-term adverse
international price fluctuations.
3.        This mechanism would ensure that the transfer price of crude
palm oil from PALMINDUSTRIE to the domestic refining industry would
closely follow international prices. The broader problems of
deregulation of consumer product prices (in particular refined oil) and
of appropriate levels of tariffs to grant the required net effective
protection to each stage of the production/processing chain would be
dealt with in the general context of SAL III negotiations to be held by
the end of 1985.
WAPA A
July 1985

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
OUTGROWER PRICE POLICY
1.        Producer prices for fresh fruit bunches (FFB) have been revised
with long Intervals and uneven rates unlike producer prices on other tree
crops (see Table 1). Following the doubling of prices in 1974 from 4 to
8 CFAF/kg. a major increase in prices came about only in 1980 when the
price Increased by 50Z from 10 CFAF to 15 CFAF/kg".
2.        The price structure is illustrated on page 4 and 5.  From the
price of crude palm oil converted by the extraction rate into m
equivalent and with the value of the palm kernel added, the costs of
collection, processing, extension, overheads are deducted. In addition,
a lump-sun debt service and a contribution to the Expansion and
Replanting Fund (FER) are deducted.
3.        PALMINDUSTRIE paid in 1983/84 the outgrowers an average price
of CFAF 18.5/kg. Apart from the base price of CFAF 15/kg, all planters
were paid a supplement of CFAF 2/kg to reflect the favorable world market
price of pals oil. In addition, all planters with sales to PALMINDUSTRIE
in excess of 5 t/ha for the year received a premium of CPAF 2/kg. For
maxImum effect the supplement and the premium were paid to the planters
at the beginning of the school-year where demand for liquidity is
greatest.
Table 1

and in addition a CPAP 2/kg premium to smallholders with yields of
5 tons/ha or more.



- 130 -
Annex 5-5
Page 2 of 5
4. The revised pricing system
The producer price system represents essentially a
consolidation and an extension of the current system. The fundamental
principles are:
(a)       the outgrower should be paid the net value of his
produce at world prices plus any tariff protection of
the sub-sector;
(b)       the price structure should provide incentive to
intensive production;
(r.)     roadside prices to the various groups of outgrowers
should be uniform;
(d)      smallholders service their debt by a cess.
5.        In the future a formula for the determination of the FFB
outgrover price would be applied. The formula would have four elements:
(i)      the value of the final output expressed in FFB
equivalent terms. This value would be calculated as
the average of the ex factory revenue from domestic
and export sales of oil palm products (crude palm
oil, kernel oil, and cake). The revenue would be
converted to equivalent FFB terms by technical
conversion factors (extraction rates);
(ii)     the agricultural, processing and transport costs and
overheads connected with FFB collection, processing
and the output marketing.
The cost will be determined on a yearly basis from
PALMINDUSTRIE's account and budgets;
(iii)    a profit margin to PALMINDUSTRIE.
This margin would be calculated as a percentage of
the difference between the revenue (i) and the
operating costs (ii);
(iv)     a guaranteed floor price;
(v)      a system of production premia.
Every Month:
The outgrower would be paid the value of this harvest at the
floor price.



- 131 -
Annex 5-5
Page 3 of 5
Every 6 or 12 months:
The difference between the net price calculated by deductions
(ii) through (iv) from (i) and the floor price would be paid to the
outgrowers, as well as a premium on production. The premium system would
be based on an average yield per ha. Production (collected by
PALMINDUSTRIE) below this average would lead to a price of the entire
production below the average net price for the period; production above
average would be paid a price above average. The production premia would
be paid in connection with a trimestrial payment of the difference
between the net and floor price.
6.        Smallholders and medium-sized plantations will be paid the same
price per kg FFB at plantation road side. However, the type and level of
services provided by PALMINDUSTRIE to the medium-sized plantations will
differ from those extended to smallholders (assistance with
land-clearing, provision of planting material, fertilizer, insecticide,
roads and road maintenance, collection of harvest, extension), and will
differ among medium-sized plantations. To the latter, the road-sized
price is essentially an instalment on the total payment of the FFB
delivered to PALID USTRIE. The individual accounts will be settled
periodically by PALMINDUSTRIE. Since the net price to medium-sized
plantations will exceed the price paid to smallholders to the extent
services differ, and transfers of FFB from one outgrower to the other is
a possibility, PALMINDUSTRIE will assure by close supervision and control
that the level of FFB sold to PALMINDUSTRIE by medium-sized plantations
corresponds to the expected level of production.
wAPA A
September 1985

IVORY COAST
FIFTH OIL PALM DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PRINCIPAL ASSUMPTIONS UNDERLYING
ECONOMIC AND_FINANCIAL_ANALYSIS
A. Economic Analy8is
1. General
(a) Period: 25 years Including 5 years plantation establishment
and 20 years of production.
(b)  Exchange Rate:         US$1 - CFAF 490.
Shadow Exchange Rate: US$1 - 588.
2.  Cost Assumjtions
(a) Project Costs: includes costs of equipment, plantation
establishment, incremental local personnel and operating costs
of plantations and PALMINDUSTRIE and loss of production due to
felling of trees under the replanting program; costs include
physical contingencies of 0 to 10 Z. (Details: Project
Working Papers; Project Cost Tables).
(b) Inputs would be purchased under predominantly competitive
conditions and no shadow pricing of domestic resources has been
done.
3. Benefits Assumptions
(a) Production: Production would commence PY4. Yield profiles
are in Annex 5-1; Production Summary in Annex 5-2.
(b)  Prices:  Economic prices of palm oil and palm kernels are
export equivalent prices based on world market prices (CIF)
less Insurance, freight and port handl.ing, and storage charges.
World market prices are forecast by IBRD (January 1985 forecast) up to
1995; beyond 1995 prices are assumed to stay constant in real terms.
B. Financial Analysis
1. General
(a) Period: 25 years Including 5 years plantation establishment and
20 years of production.



- 136 -
Annex 5-6
Page 2 of 2
(b)  Inflation factors:  as In project costing (par& 2) 1986:7.5Z,
1987-90: 8X, 1991, and thereafter: 5Z.
(c) Exchange Rate: US$1 - CFAF 490.
2.  Outflow Assup tions
(a) Payments for goods and services through the life of the project
include taxes, and are presumed to be made when cost Is
incurred for project execution.
(b) Debt service on foreign loans are estimated as follows:
IBRD: 20 years term at 9.29% p.a. variable, 5 years grace, 0.75X
commitment fee on indisbursed amount.
CCCE:  20 years term at 101 p.a.. 5 years grace*
CDC:    15 years term at 9% p.a., 5 years grace.
EDF:    40 years term at 12 p.a., 10 years grace.
EI:    15 years term at 6.6% p.a., 5 years grace.
3. Inflow Assumptions
(a) Disbursement of IBRD loan, schedule in Annex 3-9: Schedule of
Disbursement.
(b) Tax revenues are assumed to accrue on project expenditures
(Project Working Papers: Input and Project Costs).
(c)  Sales revenue is estimated assuming the timing and volume
used in the economic analysis. Prices of crude palm oil and palm
kernels are derived from world CIF prices plus export subsidy and
less insurance, freight, port, handling, and storage charges.
(Details: Annex 5-4: Markets, Prices and Demand). Price
increase assumptions are the same as those of the economic
analysis.
WAPA A
February 1985
